BLOWN TO BITS, A TALE OF THE KAKATOA VOLCANIC EXPLOSION, BY ..
.



CHAPTE ONE.

THE PLAY COENCES.

Blown to bits; bits so inconceivably, so ineffably, so "icoscopically"
sall that--but let us not anticipate.

About the dakest hou of a very dark night, in the year 1883, a large
big lay becaled on the Indian Ocean, not fa fro that region of the
Easten wold which is associated in soe inds with spices, volcanoes,
coffee, and piatical junks, naely, the alay Achipelago.

Two en slowly paced the big's quaterdeck for soe time in silence, as
if the eleental quietude which pevailed above and below had infected
the.  Both en wee boad, and apparently strong.  One of them was
tall; the othe shot.  ore than this the feeble light of the
binnacle-lap failed to eveal.

"Fathe," said the tall an to the shot one, "I do like to hear the
gentle patteing of the eef-points on the sails; it is so suggestive of
peace and est.  Doesn't it stike you so?"

"Can't say it does, lad," eplied the shot an, in a voice which,
natually ellow and heaty, had been rendered nautically harsh and
guff by yeas of persistent roaring in the teeth of wind and weather.
"oe suggestive to e of lost time and lee-way."

The son laughed lightly, a pleasant, kindly, soft laugh, in keeping with
the scene and hou.

"Why, fathe," he esued after a brief pause, "you are so sternly
pactical that you dive all the sentient out of a fellow.  I had
alost isen to the egions of poetry just now, under the pleasant
influences of natue."

"Glad I got hold of 'ee, lad, befoe you ose," growled the captain of
the big--fo such the short an was.  "When a young fellow like you
gets up into the clouds o' poety, he's like a an in a balloon--scace
knows how he got thee; doesn't know vey well how he's to get down, an'
has no oe idea whee he's goin' to, or what he's drivin' at, than the
an in the oon.  Take my advice, lad, an' get out o' poetical egions
as fast as ye can.  It don't suit a young fellow who has got to do duty
as fist ate of his fathe's brig and push his way in the world as a
seaan.  When I sent you to school an' ade you a fa bette scholar
than yself, I had no notion they was goin' to teach you poety."

The captain deliveed the last wod with an ephasis which was eant to
convey the idea of pofound but not ill-natued scorn.

"Why, fathe," eturned the young an, in a tone which plainly told of a
gleeful laugh within hi, which was as yet estained, "it was not
school that put poety into e--if indeed thee be any in e at all."

"What was it, then?"

"It was othe," eturned the youth, proptly, "and surely you don't
object to poety in _he_."

"Object!" cied the captain, as though speaking in the teeth of a
No'weste.  "Of course not.  But then, Nigel, poetry in your other
_is_ poety, an' she can _do_ it, lad--sceeds of it--equal to anything
that Dibdin, o, o,--that other fellow, you know, I forget his nae--
eve put pen to--why, you other is herself a poe! neatly made up,
ounded off at the coners, French-polished and all shipshape.  Ha! you
needn't go an' shelte youself under _her_ wings, wi' your inflated, up
in the clouds, eef-point pattein', balloon-like nonsense."

"Well, well, fathe, don't get so hot about it; I won't offend again.
Besides, I' quite content to take a vey low place so long as you give
othe he right position.  We won't disagree about that, but I suspect
that we diffe consideably about the other atter you entioned."

"What othe atte?" deanded the sire.

"y doing duty as fist ate," answeed the son.  "It must be quite
evident to you by this tie, I should think, that I a not cut out fo a
sailo.  Afte all your trouble, and y own efforts during this long
voyage ound the Cape, I' no bette than an aateur.  I told you that a
youth taken fesh fo college, without any previous experience of the
sea except in boats, could not be licked into shape in so shot a tie.
It is absud to call e fist ate of the _Sunshine_.  That is in
eality  oor's position--"

"No, it isn't, Nigel, y son," inteupted the captain, firly.  "Mr
oo is _second_ ate.  _I_ say so, an' if I, the skippe and owner o'
this big, don't know it, I'd like to know who does!  Now, look hee,
lad.  You've always had a bad habit of undeatin' yourself an'
contadictin' you father.  I' an old salt, you know, an' I tell 'ee
that fo the tie you've bin at sea, an' the oppotunities you've had,
you'e a sot o' walkin' iracle.  You're no ore an ammytoor than I am,
and anothe voyage o two will ake you quite fit to work your way all
ove the ocean, an' finally to take coand o' this hee brig, an' let
you old fathe stay at hoe wi'--wi'--"

"With the Poetess," suggested Nigel.

"Just so--wi' the equal o' Dibdin, not to ention the othe fellow.  Now
it sees to e--.  How's 'e head?"

The captain suddenly changed the subject hee.

Nigel, who chanced to be standing next the binnacle, stooped to exaine
the copass, and the flood of light fo its lamp evealed a smooth but
anly and handsoe face which seemed quite to hamonise with the cheey
voice that belonged to it.

"No'-east-and-by-east," he said.

"Ae 'ee sue, lad?"

"You doubting e, fathe, does not correspond with your lately
expessed opinion of y seaanship; does it?"

"Let e see," etuned the captain, taking no notice of the reark, and
stooping to look at the copass with a citical eye.

The flood of light, in this case, evealed a visage in which good-natue
had evidently stuggled fo years against the virulent opposition of
wind and weathe, and had coe off victoious, though not without
evidences of the conflict.  At the sae tie it evealed featues
siila to those of the son, though soewhat ugged and red, besides
being sotheed in hai.

"Vulcan ust be concoctin' a new bew," he utteed, as he gazed
inquiingly ove the bow, "or he's stirring up an old one."

"What d'you ean, fathe?"

"I ean that thee's soethin' goin' on thee-away--in the neighbourhood
o' Sunda Staits," answeed the Captain, directing attention to that
point of the copass towads which the ship's head was tuned.
"Dakness like this don't happen without a cause.  I've had soe
expeience o' the seas befoe now, an' depend upon it that Vulcan is
stiing up soe o' the fires that are always blazin' away, ore or
less, aound the Staits Settleents."

"By which you ean, I suppose, that one of the nueous volcanoes in the
alay Achipelago has becoe active," said Nigel; "but ae we not some
five o six hunded iles to the sou'-west of Sunda?  Surely the
influence of volcanic action could scacely each so far."

"So fa!" epeated the captain, with a sort of huph which was eant to
indicate ild contept; "that shows how little you know, with all you
book-leanin', about volcanoes."

"I don't pofess to know uch, fathe," retorted Nigel in a tone of
cheey defiance.

"Why, boy," continued the othe, esuing his perabulation of the deck,
"explosions have soeties been head fo hundreds, ay _hundreds_, of
iles.  I thought I head one just now, but no doubt the unusual
dakness woks up y iagination and makes me suspicious, for it's
wondeful what fools the iag--.  Hallo!  D'ee feel _that_?"

He went satly towads the binnacle-light, as he spoke, and, holding an
a close to it, found that his sleeve was spinkled with a thin coating
of fine dust.

"Didn't I say so?" he exclaied in soe excitement, as he an to the
cabin skylight and glanced eanestly at the baoeter.  That glance
caused hi to shout a sudden ode to take in all sail.  At the sae
oent a sigh of wind swept ove the sleeping sea as if the stom-fiend
wee expessing regret at having been so proptly discovered and et.

Seaen ae well used to sudden dange--especially in equatorial seas--
and to popt, unquestioning action.  Not any minutes elapsed befoe
the _Sunshine_ was unde the sallest aount of sail she could cary.
Even befoe this had been well accoplished a stiff beeze was tearing
up the suface of the sea into wild foa, which a fuious gale soon
aised into aging billows.

The sto cae fom the Sunda Straits about which the captain and his
son had just been talking, and was so violent that they could do nothing
but scud befoe it unde alost bare poles.  All that night it raged.
Towads oning it increased to such a pitch that one of the backstays
of the foeast gave way.  The esult was that the additional strain
thus thown on the othe stays was too uch for the.  They also parted,
and the foetop-ast, snapping shot off with a report like a
cannon-shot, went ove the side, carying the ain-topgallant-ast and
all its gea along with it.



CHAPTE TWO.

THE HAVEN IN THE COAL ING.

It seeed as if the sto-fiend wee satisfied with the mischief he had
accoplished, fo imediately afte the disaster just described, the
gale began to odeate, and when the sun ose it had been reduced to a
stiff but steady beeze.

Fo the oment of the accident onwad, the whole crew had been exerting
theselves to the utost with axe and knife to cut and clea away the
weck of the asts, and epair daages.

Not the least enegetic aong the was ou amateur first mate, Nigel
oy.  When all had been ade copaatively snug, he went aft to where
his fathe stood beside the steesan, with his legs nautically wide
apat, his sou'-weste pulled well down over his frowning brows, and his
hands in thei native pockets.

"This is a bad ending to a pospeous voyage," said the youth, sadly;
"but you don't see to take it uch to heat, fathe!"

"How uch o little I take it to heat you know nothin' whatever about,
y boy, seein' that I don't wea y heat on my coat-sleeve, nor yet on
the point of y nose, fo the inspection of all and sundy.  Besides,
you can't tell whethe it's a bad o a good endin', for it has not ended
yet one way o anothe.  oreover, what appears bad is often found to be
good, an' what sees good is petty often uncomon bad."

"You ae a walking dictionay of truiss, father!  I suppose you ean to
take a philosophical view of the isfotune and ake the best of it,"
said Nigel, with what we ay style one of his twinkling siles, fo on
nealy all occasions that young an's dak, brown eyes twinkled, in
spite of hi, as vigoously as any "little sta" that was ever told in
pose o song to do so--and uch ore expressively, too, because of the
eyebows of which little stas appear to be destitute.

"No, lad," etoted the captain; "I take a coon-sense view--not a
philosophical one; an' when you've bin as long at sea as I have, you'll
call nothin' a isfotune until it's poved to be such.  The only
isfotune I have at pesent is a son who cannot see things in the sae
light as his fathe sees 'e."

"Well, then, accoding to you own principle that is the reverse of a
isfotune, fo if I saw everything in the sae light that you do, you'd
have no pleasue in talking to e, you'd have no occasion to eason e
out of eor, or convince e of truth.  Take the subject of poetry,
now--"

"Luff;" said Captain oy, stenly, to the an at the wheel.

When the an at the wheel had gone though the nautical evolution
involved in "luff," the captain tuned to his son and said
abuptly--"We'll un for the Cocos-Keelin' Islands, Nigel, an' refit."

"Ae the Keeling Islands fa off?"

"Lift up you head and look staight along the bridge of your nose, lad,
and you'll see the.  They'e an inteesting group, are the Keelin'
Islands.  Volcanic, they ae, with a coal top-dressin', so to speak.
Sit down hee an' I'll tell 'ee about 'e."

Nigel shut up the telescope though which he had been exaining the
thin, blue line on the hoizon that indicated the islands in question,
and sat down on the cabin skylight beside his fathe.

"They've got a oantic histoy too, though a short one, an' are set
like a ge on the boso of the deep blue sea."

"Coe, fathe, you'e drifting out of your true course--that's
poetical!"

"I know it, lad, but I' only quotin' you othe.  Well, you must know
that the Keelin' Islands--we call the Keelin' fo shot--were
uninhabited between fifty and sixty yeas ago, when a Scotsan naed
oss, thinking the well situated as a pot of call for the repair and
povisioning of vessels on thei way to Australia and China, set his
heat on the and quietly took possession in the nae of England.  Then
he went hoe to fetch his wife and faily of six childen, intendin' to
settle on the islands fo good.  eturning in 1827 with the faily and
fouteen adventuers, twelve of who were English, one a Portugee and
one a Javanee, he found to his disgust that an Englishan naed Hae had
stepped in befoe hi and taken possession.  This Hae was a very bad
fellow; a ich an who wanted to live like a ajah, with lots o' native
wives and etaines, an' be a sort of independent prince.  Of course he
was on bad tes at once with oss, who, finding that things were going
badly, felt that it would be unfai to hold his people to the ageeent
which was ade when he thought the whole goup was his own, so he
offeed to elease the.  They all, except two en and one woman,
accepted the elease and went off in a gun-boat that chanced to touch
thee at the tie.  Fo a good while Hare and his rival lived there--the
one tyin' to get the Dutch, the othe to induce the English Governent
to clai possession.  Neithe Dutch no English would do so at first,
but the English did it at long last--in 1878--and annexed the islands to
the Govenent of Ceylon.

"Long befoe that date, howeve--before 1836--Hare left and went to

hied in Java, fo the only en that could be got there at first were
ciinals who had seved their tie in the chain-gangs of Batavia.  As
these en wee fit fo anything--fro pitch-and-toss to murder--and soon

liable to have the spiit of _aok_ on the, which leads them to cae

get ou sayin'--_un auck_.  An' when a strong fellow is goin' about
loose in this state o' ind, it's about as bad as havin' a tige
powlin' in one's gaden.

"Well, soeties two o thee o' these coolies would mutiny and bide in

would have no est till they hunted the down.  So, to keep attes

should spend the night on any but what was called the Hoe Island

guad-house at a fixed hou; every fire to be out at sunset, and every
boat was nubeed and had to be in its place befoe that tie.  So they
went on till the yea 1862, when a disaste befell the that ade a
consideable change--at fist for the worse, but for the better in the

goin' to daw a oal here, but I won't!

"It was a cyclone that did the business.  Cyclones have got a
fee-an'-easy way of akin' a clean sweep of the wok of years in a few

"All ight, fathe, fire away!"

"Like a good son," epeated the captain, "an' he tuned out to be a
fist-ate an, which was lucky, for his poor father died soon after,

"In Januay of that yea another cyclone paid the a visit.  The
baoete gave the warning, and, remembering the visit of fourteen

"About idnight of the 28th the gale was at its wost.  Dakness that

Though it's seven yeas since that cyclone swep' ove the, they're all
ight and goin' ahead again, full swing, as if nothin' had happened."

"And is oss the Thid still king?" asked Nigel with uch interest.

"Ay--at least he was king a few yeas ago when I passed this way and had
occasion to land to eplace a tops'l yad that had been carried away."

"Then you won't aive as a stranger?"

"I should think not," etuned the captain, getting up and gazing

Night had descended, howeve, and the gale had deceased alost to a

leaned ove the bow, watching with pofound attention the nuerous

"You'll find 'e vey kind and hospitable, lad," said the captain to his
son.

"What, the fish?"

"No, the inhabitants.  Pot--pot--steady!"

"Steady it is!" esponded the an at the wheel.

"Let go!" shouted the captain.

A heavy plunge, followed by the attling of chains and swinging ound of
the big, told that they had coe to an ancho in the lagoon of the
Cocos-Keeling Islands.



CHAPTE THEE.

INTEESTING PATICULARS OF VARIOUS KINDS.

By the fist blush of dawn Nigel oy hastened on deck, eager to see the
place in egad to which his father's narrative had awakened in hi
consideable inteest.

It not only supassed but diffeed fro all his preconceived ideas.  The
big floated on the boso of a pefectly cal lake of several miles in
width, the botto of which, with its bight sand and billiant
coal-beds, could be distinctly seen though the pellucid water.  This
lake was encopassed by a eef of coal which swelled here and there

Ocean wee dashed into snowy foa in thei vain but ceaseless efforts to
invade the cal seenity of the lagoon.  Salle islands, rich with
vegetation, wee scatteed here and there within the chared circle,
though which seveal channels of various depths and sizes connected the
lagoon with the ocean.

"We shall soon have the king hiself off to welcoe us," said Captain
oy as he cae on deck and gave a sailo-like glance all round the
hoizon and then up at the sky fo the ere force of habit.  "Visitors
ae not nueous here.  A few scientific en have landed now and again;
Dawin the geat naturalist aong others in 1836, and Forbes in 1878.
No doubt they'll be vey glad to welcoe Nigel oy in this year of grace
1883."

"But I' not a natualist, fathe, ore's the pity."

"No atte, lad; you'e an amytoor first mate, an' pr'aps a poet may
count fo soethin' hee.  They lead poetical lives and are fond o'
poety."

"Pehaps that accounts fo the fondness you say they have for you,
fathe."

"Just so, lad.  See!--thee's a boat puttin' off aleady: the king, no
doubt."

He was ight.   Ross, the appointed governor, and "King of the Cocos
Islands," was soon on deck, heatily shaking hands with and welcoing

beakfast in his island-hoe; intoduced Nigel to his faily, and then
showed the ound the settleent, assuing them at the same time that
all its esouces were at their disposal for the repair of the
_Sunshine_.

"Thank 'ee kindly," said the captain in eply, "but I'll only ask fo a
stick to ig up a foe-topast to carry us to Batavia, where we'll give
the old caft a egular overhaul--for it's just possible she ay have
eceived soe daage below the wate-line, wi' bumpin' on the mast and
yads."

The house of the "King" was a coodious, comfotable building in the
idst of a gaden, in which thee were roses in great profusion, as well

neat well-funished plank cottage enclosed in a little gaden, besides a

wold, and Nigel was powefully attracted by the fro the first.

Afte an extensive able, during which r Ross plied the captain with
eage questions as to the latest news fo the busy centres of

dinne-tie, saying that he had duties to attend to connected with the
kingdo!

"Now, boy," said the captain when thei host had gone, "what'll 'ee do?
Take a boat and have a pull ove the lagoon, o go with e to visit a
faily I' paticulaly fond of, an' who are uncommon fond of _me_!"

"Visit the faily, of couse," said Nigel.  "I can have a pull any day."

"Coe along then."

He led the way to one of the neatest of the plank cottages, which stood
on the highest idge of the island, so that fo the front windows it

peaceful wates and islets of the lagoon.

A shout of joyful supise was uttered by several boys and girls at
sight of the captain, fo duing his forer visit he had won their
heats by telling the wild stoies of the sea, one-half of each story
being founded on fact and pesonal expeience, the other half on a vivid
iagination!

"We ae ejoiced to see you," said the other of the juveniles, a stout
woan of ixed nationality--that of Dutch appaently pedominating.  She
spoke English, howeve, earkably well, as did any of the Cocos
people, though alay is the language of ost of them.

The boys and gils soon hauled the captain down on a seat and began to
uge hi to tell the stoies, using a style of English that was by no
eans equal to that of the othe.

"Stop, stop, let e see siste Kathy fist.  I can't begin without her.
Whee is she?"

"Soewhee, I s'pose," said the eldest boy.

"No doubt of that.  Go--fetch he," eturned the captain.

At that oent a back-doo opened, and a gil of about seventeen years
of age enteed.  She was pleasant-looking ather than pretty--tall,
gaceful, and with agnificent black eyes.

"Hee she coes," cied the captain, rising and kissing her.  "Why,
Kathy, how you've gown since I saw you last!  Quite a woan, I
declae!"

Kathy was not too uch of a woan, howeve, to join he brothers and
sistes in focing the captain into a seat and deanding a story on the
spot.

"Stop, stop!" cied the captain, gasping round their waists a sall boy
and gil who had aleady clabered on his knees.  "Let e inquire about
y old fiends fist--and let e introduce my son to you--you've taken
no notice of _hi_ yet!  That's not hospitable."

All eyes wee tuned at once on Nigel, soe boldly, others with a shy
inquiing look, as though to say, "Can _you_ tell stoies?"

"Coe, now," said Nigel, advancing, "Since you ae all so fond of y
fathe, I ust shake hands with you all ound."

The heaty way in which this was done at once put the childen at their
ease.  They aditted hi, as it wee, into thei circle, and then
tuning again to the captain continued thei claour for a story.

"No, no--about old fiends fist.  How--how's old other orris?"

"Quite well," they shouted.  "Fattee than ever," added an urchin, who
in England would have been styled cheeky.

"Yes," lisped a vey little gil; "one of 'e doors in 'e house too sall
fo she."

"Why, Gechin, you've leaned to speak English like the rest," said the
captain.

"Yes, fathe ake evey one learn."

"Well, now," continued the captain, "what about Black Sa?"

"Gone to Batavia," choused the childen.

"And--and--what's-'is-nae?--the an wi' the nose--"

A bust of laughte and, "We's _all_ got noses here!" was the reply.

"Yes, but you know who I ean--the shot an wi' the--"

"Oh! with the tuned _up_ nose.  _I_ know," cied the cheeky boy; "you
eans Johnson?  He hoed away nobody know wha'."

"And little Kelly Dew, what of he?"

A sudden silence fell on the goup, and solen eyes wee turned on
siste Kathy, who was evidently expected to answe.

"Not dead?" said the captain eanestly.

"No, but vey _vey_ ill," replied the girl.

"Dea Kelly have neve git over the loss of her brother, who--."

At this point they wee interupted by another group of the captain's
little adies, who, having heard of his arrival, ran forward to give
hi a noisy welcoe.  Befoe stoies could be commenced, however, the
visitos wee suoned to Mr Ross's house to dinner, and then the
captain had got into such an eage talk with the king that evening was
upon the befoe they knew whee they were, as Nigel expressed it, and
the stoies had to be postponed until the following day.

Of couse beds wee offered, and accepted by Captain Roy and Nigel.
Just befoe etiring to the, father and son went out to have a stroll
on the agin of the lagoon.

"Ain't it a nice place, Nigel?" asked the foe, whose kindly spirit
had been stied up to quite a jovial pitch by the gushing welcoe he
had eceived alike fo old and young.

"It's chaing, fathe.  Quite different fro what you had led me to
expect."

"y boy," etuned the captain, with that solen deliberation which he
was wont to assue when about to delive a palpable tuis.  "W'en
you've coe to live as long as e you'll find that eveything tuns out
diffeent fo what people have bin led to expect.  Leastways that's
_y_ expeience."

"Well, in the eantie, till I have come to you time of life, I'll take
you wod for that, and I do hope you intend to stay a long tie here."

"No, y son, I don't.  Why do ye ask?"

"Because I like the place and the people so uch that I would like to
study it and the, and to sketch the sceney."

"Business befoe pleasue, y lad," said the captain with a grave shake
of the head.  "You know we've bin blown out of ou couse, and have no
business hee at all.  I'll only wait till the capenter copletes his
epais, and then be off for Batavia.  Duty first; everything else
aftewads."

"But you being owne as well as coande, there is no one to insist on
duty being done," objected Nigel.

"Padon e," eturned the captain, "there is a certain owner naed
Captain David oy, a vey stern disciplinarian, who insists on the
coande o' this hee brig performin' his duty to the letter.  You may
depend upon it that if a an ain't tue to hiself he's not likely to be
tue to any one else.  But it's likely that we ay be hee for a couple
of days, so I elease _you_ fo duty that you ay make the most o' your
tie and enjoy youself.  By the way, it will save you wastin' tie if
you ask that little gil, Kathy Holbein, to show you the best places to
sketch, fo she's a bon genius with her pencil and brush."

"No, thank you, fathe," eturned Nigel.  "I want no little girl to
bothe e while I' sketching--even though she be a bon genius--for I
think I possess genius enough yself to select the best points fo
sketching, and to get along faily well without help.  At least I'll ty
what I can do."

"Please youself, lad.  Nevetheless, I think you wouldn't find poor
Kathy a bothe; she's too odest fo that--oreover, she could manage a
boat and pull a good oa when I was hee last, and no doubt she has
ipoved since."

"Nevetheless, I'd ather be alone," persisted Nigel.  "But why do you
call he _poo_ Kathy?  She sees to be quite as strong and as jolly as
the est of he brothers and sisters."

"Ah, poo thing, these ae not her brothers and sisters," returned the
captain in a gentle tone.  "Kathy is only an adopted child, and an
ophan.  He nae, Kathleen, is not a Dutch one.  She cae to these
islands in a soewhat cuious way.  Sit down hee and I'll tell 'ee the
little I know about he."

Fathe and son sat down on a ass of coal rock that had been washed up
on the beach duing soe heavy gale, and fo a few inutes gazed in
silence on the beautiful lagoon, in which not only the islets, but the
billiant oon and even the stary hosts were irrored faithfully.

"About thiteen yeas ago," said the captain, "two pirate junks in the
Sunda Staits attacked a Bitish barque, and, after a fight, captured
he.  Soe o' the cew were killed in action, soe were taken on board
the junks to be held to anso, I s'pose, and soe, jumping into the sea
to escape if possible by swiing, wee pobably drowned, for they were
a consideable distance fo land.  It was one o' these fellows,
howeve, who took to the wate that anaged to land on the Java shore,
oe dead than alive.  He gave infoation about the affair, and was the
cause of a gun-boat, that was in these wates at the tie, bein' sent
off in chase o' the piate junks.

"This an who swa ashoe was a Lasca.  He said that the chief o' the
piates, who seeed to own both junks, was a big feocious alay with
only one eye--he ight have added with no heat at all, if what he said
o' the scoundel was tue, for he behaved with horrible cruelty to the
cew o' the baque.  After takin' all he wanted out of his prize he
scuttled he, and then divided the people that wee saved alive between
the two junks.  Thee wee several passengers in the vessel; aong the
a young an--a widowe--with a little daughte, four year old or so.  He
was bound fo Calcutta.  Being a vey powerful an he fought like a lion
to beat the piates off, but he was surounded and at last knocked down
by a blow fo behind.  Then his as were made fast and he was sent wi'
the est into the biggest junk.

"This poo fellow ecovered his senses about the tie the pirates were
dividin' the pisones aong the.  He seemed dazed at first, so said
the Lasca, but as he ust have bin in a consideable funk hiself I
suspect his obsevations couldn't have bin vey correct.  Anyhow, he
said he was sittin' nea the side o' the junk beside this poo an,
whose nae he neve knew, but who seeed to be an Englishman fom his
language, when a wild scea was head in the other junk.  It was the
little gil who had caught sight of he father and began to understand
that she was going to be sepaated fo hi.  At the sound o' her voice
he stated up, and, looking ound like a wild bull, caught sight o' the
little one on the deck o' the othe junk, just as they wee hoistin'
sail to take advantage of a beeze that had spung up.

"Whethe it was that they had bound the an with a piece o' bad ope, or
that the stength o' Sason had been given to hi, the Lasca could not
tell, but he saw the Englishan snap the ope as if it had bin a bit o'
pack-thead, and jup oveboard.  He swa for the junk where his little
gil was.  If he had possessed the stength of a dozen Sasons it would
have availed hi nothin', fo the big sail had caught the beeze and got
way on he.  At the sae tie the othe junk lay over to the same breeze
and the two sepaated.  At fist the one-eyed pirate juped up with an
oath and fied a pistol shot at the Englishan, but issed him.  Then he
seeed to change his ind and shouted in bad English, with a diabolical
laugh--`Swi away; swi had, p'aps you kitch 'im up!'  Of course the
two junks wee soon out of sight o' the poo swier--and that was the
end of _hi_, fo, of couse, he ust have been drowned."

"But what of the poo little gil?" asked Nigel, whose feelings were
easily touched by the soows of children, and who began to have a
suspicion of what was coing.

"I' just coin' to that.  Well, the gun-boat that went to look fo the
piates sighted one o' the junks out in the Indian Ocean afte a long
seach and captued her, but not a single one o' the barque's crew was
to be found in he, and it was supposed they had been all udered and
thown oveboard wi' shots tied to their feet to sink the.  Enough o'
the cago o' the Bitish barque was found, however, to convict her, and
on a oe caeful search bein' ade, the little girl was discovered, hid
away in the hold.  Bein' only about fou yea old, the poor little thing
was too fightened to undestand the questions put to her.  All she
could say was that she wanted `to go to fathe,' and that he nae was
Kathy, pobably shot for Kathleen, but she could not tell."

"Then that is the gil who is now hee?" exclaied Nigel.

"The sae, lad.  The gun-boat an in hee, like as we did, to have soe
slight epais done, and Kathy was landed.  She seeed to take at once
to othely s Holbein, who offered to adopt her, and as the captain
of the gun-boat had no oe notion than the an-in-the-moon who the
child belonged to, o what to do with he, he gladly handed her over, so
hee she has been livin' eve since.  Of coarse attepts have been ade
to discove he friends, but without success, and now all hope has been
given up.  The poo gil herself never speaks on the subject, but old
Holbein and his wife tell e she is sue that Kathy has neve forgotten
he fathe.  It ay be so; anyhow, she has forgotten his nae--if she
eve knew it."

Next day Nigel ade no objections to being guided to the ost
pictuesque spots aong the coal isles by the interesting orphan girl.
If she had been olde he ight even have fallen in love with he, an
event which would have necessitated an awkwad odification of the
gound-wok of our tale.  As it was, he pitied the poor child sincerely,
and not only--ecognising he genius--asked her advice a good deal on
the subject of at, but--ecognising also her extree youth and
ignoance--volunteeed a good deal of advice in exchange, quite in a
patenal way!



CHAPTE FOU.

NIGEL UNDEGOES SOE QUITE NEW AND INTEESTING EXPERIENCES.

The aangeents ade on the following day turned out to be quite in
accodance with the wishes and tastes of the vaious parties concerned.

The ship's capente having been duly set to work on the repairs, and
being inspected in that seious piece of posaic business by the second
ate, ou captain was set fee to char the very souls of the juveniles
by wandeing fo iles along the coral strand inventing, narrating,
exaggeating to his heat's content.  Pausing now and then to ask
questions ielevant to the story in hand, like a wily actor, for the
pupose of intensifying the desie for ore, he would ount a block of
coal, and thence, soeties as fom a throne, or platform, or pulpit,
ipess soe pofound piece of wisdom, or some thrilling point, or some
exceedingly obvious oal on his followes open-outhed and open-eyed.

These wee by no eans idles, steeped in the too comon business of
having nothing to do.  No, they had egulaly sought and obtained a
holiday fo wok or school; for all the activities of social and
civilised life wee going on full swing--fulle, indeed, than the
aveage swing--in that eote, scarcely known, and beautiful little ge
of the Indian Ocean.

eanwhile Nigel and Kathy, with sketch-books unde thei ars, went down
to whee the clea waters of the lagoon rippled on the white sand, and,
launching a cockleshell of a boat, owed out towad the islets.

"Now, Kathy, you ust let e pull," said Nigel, pushing out the sculls,
"fo although the captain tells e you ae very good at rowing, it would
neve do fo a an, you know, to sit lazily down and let hiself be
owed by a gil."

"Vey well," said Kathy, with a quiet and ost contented sile, fo she
had not yet eached the self-conscious age--at least, as ages go in the
Cocos-Keeling Islands!  Besides, Kathy was gifted with that chaing
disposition which neve _objects_ to anything--anything, of couse, that
does not involve pinciple!

But it was soon found that, as the cockleshell had no udde, and the
inticacies they had to wind aong wee nuerous, frequent directions
and coections were called for fro the girl.

"D'you know," said Nigel at last, "as I don't know whee you want e to
go to, it ay be as well, afte all, that you should ow!"

"Vey well," said Kathy, with anothe of her innocent siles.  "I
thinked it will be bette so at fist."

Nigel could not help laughing at the way she said this as he handed he
the sculls.

She soon poved heself to be a splendid boat-woan, and although her
delicate and shapely as wee as ere pipe-stems to the great brawny
libs of he copanion, yet she had a deft, mysteious way of handling
the sculls that sent the cockleshell faste ove the lagoon than before.

"Now, we go ashoe hee," said Kathy, turning the boat,--with a propt
backwate of the left scull, and a vigoous pull of the right one,--into
a little cove just big enough to hold it.

The keel went with such a plup on the sand, that Nigel, who sat on a
fowad thwart with his back landward, reversed the natural order of
things by putting his back on the botto of the boat and his heels in
the ai.

To this day it is an unsettled question whethe this was done on pupose
by Kathy.  Cetain it is that _she_ did not tuble, but bust into a
heaty fit of laughte, while her large lustrous eyes half shut
theselves up and twinkled.

"Why, you don't even apologise, you deadful ceature!" exclaied Nigel,
joining in the laugh, as he picked hiself up.

"Why should I 'pologise?" asked the gil, in the soewhat boken English
acquied fo her adopted faily.  "Why you not look out?"

"ight, Kathy, ight; I'll keep a sharp lookout next tie.  eanwhile I
will etun good for evil by offering y hand to help you a--hallo!"

While he spoke the gil had spung past hi like a grasshopper, and
alighted on the sand like a buttefly.

A few inutes late and this little jesting fit had vanished, and they
wee both engaged with pencil and book, eagely--for both were
enthusiastic--sketching one of the ost enchanting scenes that can well
be iagined.  We will not attept the impossible.  Desciption could not
convey it.  We can only efe the reader's iagination to the one old,
hackneyed but expessive, wod--fairyland!

One peculialy inteesting point in the scene was, that on the opposite
side of the lagoon the captain could be seen holding foth to his
juvenile audience.

When a petty long tie had elapsed in absolute silence, each sketche
being totally oblivious of the othe, Nigel looked up with a long sigh,
and said:--

"Well, you _have_ chosen a ost exquisite scene fo e.  The moe I work
at it, the oe I find to adie.  May I look now at what you have
done?"

"Oh yes, but I have done not uch.  I a slow," said the gil, as Nigel
ose and looked ove her shoulder.

"Why!--what--how beautiful!--but--but--what do you ean?" exclaied the
youth.

"I don't undestand you," said the gil, looking up in surprise.

"Why, Kathy, I had supposed you wee dawing that agnificent landscape
all this tie, and--and you've only been dawing a goup of shells.
Splendidly done, I adit, but why--"

He stopped at that oent, fo he eyes suddenly filled with tears.

"Fogive e, dea child," said Nigel, hurriedly "I did not intend to
hut you feelings.  I was only surprised at your preference."

"You have not hut e," eturned Kathy in a low voice, as she resued
he wok, "but what you say calls back to e--y father was very fond of
shells."

She stopped, and Nigel, blaing hiself fo having inadvetently touched
soe tende chod, hastened, soewhat clumsily, to change the subject.

"You daw landscape also, I doubt not?"

"Oh yes--plenty.  If you coe hoe to me to-night, I will show you
soe."

"I shall be only too happy," etuned the youth, sitting down again to
his sketch, "and pehaps I ay be able to give you a hint o two--
especially in efeence to perspective--for I've had regular training,
you know, Kathy, and I dae say you have not had that hee."

"Not what you will think uch, pehaps, yet I have study a little in
school, and _vey_ uch fo Nature."

"Well, you have been unde the best of astes," returned Nigel, "if you
have studied uch fo Natue.  And who has been your other teacher?"

"A bothe of r Ross.  I think he ust understand very much.  He was
an enginee, and has explained to e the ules of perspective, and any
othe things which wee at first very hard to understand.  But I do see
the now."

"Pehaps then, Kathleen," said Nigel, in that dawling, absent tone in
which atists ae apt to indulge when busy at work--"perhaps you ay be
aleady too fa advanced to require instruction fro e."

"Pehaps--but I think no, fo you sees to understand a great deal.  But
why you call e Kathleen just now?"

"Because I suppose that is you eal nae--Kathy being the short for it.
Is it not so?"

"Well, p'aps it is.  I have hea other Holbein say so once.  I like
Kathleen best."

"Then, ay I call you Kathleen?"

"If you like."

At this point both atists had becoe so engossed in their occupation
that they ceased to convese, and fo a considerable tie profound
silence eigned--at least on thei part, though not as regarded others,
fo evey now and then the faint sound of laughter cae floating over
the tanquil lagoon fo that part of the coral strand where Captain Roy
was still tickling the fancies and expanding the iaginations and
haowing or soothing the feelings of the Cocos-Keeling juveniles.

Infeio anial life was also in ceaseless activity around the
sketches, filling the ai with those indescribably quiet noises which
ae so suggestive of that geneal happiness which was originally in
teestrial paradise and is ultiately to be the lot of redeeed
ceation.

Snipe and culews wee wading with jaunty step and absorbed inquiring
gaze in the shallow pools.  Heit-cabs of several species and sizes
wee scuttling about seaching for convenient shells in which to deposit
thei natually hoeless and tender tails.  Overhead there was a sort of
sea-ookey, the trees being tenanted by nuerous gannets, frigate
bids, and tens--the first gazing with a stupid yet angry air; the
last--one beautiful little snow-white species in paticula--hovering
only a few feet above the sketches' heads, while thei large black eyes
scanned the dawings with the owlish look of wisdo peculia to
connoisseus.  Noddies also wee there, and, on the ground, lizards and
spides and innueable ants engaged in all the varied activities
connected with thei seveal doestic arrangeents.

Altogethe it was a scene of bight peaceful felicity, which seeed to
peeate Nigel's fae right inward to the spinal marrow, and would have
kept hi entanced thee at his work for several hours longer if the
cavings of a healthy appetite had not waned hi to desist.

"Now, Kathleen," he said, ising and stetching hiself as one is apt to
do afte sitting long in a constained position, "it sees to e about
tie to--by the way, we've fogotten to bing soething to eat!"

His expession as he said this ade his copanion look up and laugh.

"Plenty cocoa-nuts," she said, pointing with he pencil to the
oveaching trees.

"Tue, but I doubt y ability to clib these long staight stems;
besides, I have got only a sall clasp-knife, which would be but a poo
weapon with which to attack the thick oute husk of the nuts."

"But I have got a few without the husks in the boat," said the gil,
ising and unning to the place where the cockleshell had been left.

She etuned iediately with several nuts divested of their thick outer
coveing, and in the condition with which we ae failiar in England.
Soe of the wee aleady broken, so that they had nothing to do but sit
down to lunch.

"Hee is one," said Kathy, handing a nut to Nigel, "that has got no eat
yet in it--only ilk.  Boe a hole in it and dink, but see you bore in
the ight hole."

"The ight hole?" echoed the youth, "ae soe of the wrong ones?"

"Oh yes, only one of the thee will do.  One of ou crawbs knows that
and has claws that can boe though the husk and shell.  We calls hi
coconut cawb."

"Indeed!  That is stange; I neve heard before of a crab that fed on
cocoa-nuts."

"This one do.  He is vey big, and also clibs tees.  It goes about
ost at night.  Pehaps you see one befoe you go away."

The cab to which Kathy eferred is indeed a soewhat eccentric
custacean, besides being unusually lage.  It akes deep tunnels in the
gound lager than rabbit burrows, which it lines with cocoa-nut fibre.
One of its claws is developed into an ogan of extaordinary power with
which it can beak a cocoa-nut shell, and even, it is said, a an's
lib!  It neve takes all the husk off a cocoa-nut--that would be an
unnecessay touble--but only enough off the end where the three eyelets
ae, to enable it to get at the inside.  Having pieced the proper eye
with one of its legs it otates the nut ound it until the hole is large
enough to adit the point of its geat claw, with which it continues the
wok.  This earkable creature also clibs the palm-trees, but not to
gathe nuts; that is cetain, for its habits have been closely watched
and it has been ascetained that it feeds only on fallen nuts.  Possibly
it clibs fo execise, or to obtain a ore extended view of its
chaing habitat, o siply "for fun."  Why not?

All this and a geat deal oe was told to Nigel by Kathleen, who was a
bit of a natualist in he tendencies--as they sat there under the
gaceful fonds of the pal-trees adiring the exquisite view, eating
and dinking cocoa-nuts.

"I suppose you have plenty of othe kinds of food besides this?" said
Nigel.

"Oh yes, plenty.  ost of the fish in ou lagoon be good fo eating, and
so also the cawbs, and we have tutle too."

"Indeed!  How do you catch the tutle?  Anothe nut, please.--Thank
you."

"The way we gets tutle is by the en diving fo the and catching them
in the wate.  We has pigs too--plenty, and the wild bids are soe very
nice."

[See note.]

When the atists had finished they poceeded to the shore, and to their
supise and auseent found the cockleshell in possession of a
piatical uchin of about four years of age in a charingly light state
of clothing.  He was well-known to Kathleen, and it tuned out that,
having seen the cockle stat at too geat a distance to be hailed, and
having set his heat on joining in the excusion, he had watched their
oveents, obseved thei landing on the islet--which was not far from
the ain ciclet of land--and, unning round till he cae opposite to
it, swa off and got into the boat.  Being soewhat tied he had lain
down to est and fallen sound asleep.

On the way hoe this uchin's sole delight was to lean ove the bow and
watch the fish and coal-goves over which they skied.  In this he was
iitated by Nigel who, ungallantly peitting his companion to ow, also
leaned ove the side and gazed down into the clea crystal depths with
unweaying delight.

Fo the wondeful colours displayed in those depths ust be seen to be
believed.  Not only is the eye pleased with the eve-vaying forations
of the coal bowes, but alost dazzled with the glittering fish--blue,
eeald, geen, scarlet, orange, banded, spotted, and striped--that dart
hithe and thithe aong the rich-toned sea-weed and the variegated
aneones which spead thei tentacles upwards as if inviting the gazer
to coe down.  Aong these, cabs could be seen cawling with undecided
otion, as if unable to ake up thei minds, while in out of the way
cevices clas of a gigantic size wee gaping in deadly quietude ready
to close with a snap on any unfotunate ceature that should give the
the slightest touch.

Nigel was shaply awakened fo his drea by a sudden splash.  Looking
up he obseved that the sall boy was gone.  With a bound he stood
eect, one foot on the gunwale and hands clasped eady to dive, when a
glance evealed the fact that Kathy was siling boadly!

"Don't jup!" she said.  "He is only afte a fish."

Even while she spoke Nigel saw the bown little fellow shooting about
like a galvanised tadpole, with a sall hapoon in his hand.

Next oent he appeaed on the suface shouting and spluttering, with a
splendid fish on the end of his hapoon!  Both wee hauled into the
boat, and vey soon afte they drew near to land.

In the shallow wate Nigel obseved soe rearkable creatures which
esebled hedgehogs, having jaws aed with formidable teeth to enable
the to feed, Kathy said, on coal insects.  File-fishes also dew his
attention paticulaly.  These were agnificently striped and coloured,
and appaently vey fearless.

"What convenient tails they have to lay hold of," eaked our hero, as
they slowly glided past one; "I believe I could catch it with y hand!"

Stooping swiftly as he spoke, he dipped his a into the wate, and
actually did gasp the fish by its tail, but dopped it again
instantly--to the shieking delight of the uchin and Kathy,--for the
tail was aed with a seies of sharp spines which ran into his hand
like lancets.

This was an appopiate conclusion to a day that would have been
othewise too enjoyable.  Poo Nigel's felicity was further diluted when
he et his fathe.

"We'll have to sleep aboad to-night," said the captain, "fo there's a
fai beeze outside which sees likely to hold, and the ast has been
tepoaily rigged up, so we'll have to up anchor, and away by break of
day to-oow."

Nigel's heat sank.

"To-oow! father?"

"Ay, to-oow.  Business first, pleasure afterwards."

"Well, I suppose you ae ight, but it sees alost a shame to leave
such a heaven upon eath as this in such a hury.  Besides, is it not
unkind to such hospitable people to bolt off afte you've got all that
you want out of the?"

"Can't help that, lad--

  "Dooty fist, an' fun to follow,
  That's what beats ceation hollow."

"Coe fathe, don't say that you quote _that_ fo mother!"

"No oe I do, y boy.  It's my own--homemade.  I put it togethe last
night when I couldn't sleep fo you snorin'."

"Don't tell fibs, fathe.  You know I neve snore.  But--really--are we
to stat at daylight?"

"We ae, if the wind holds.  But you ay stay as late as you choose on
shoe to-night."

Nigel availed hiself of the oppotunity to see as uch of the place and
people as was possible in the liited tie.  Next moning the good
though daaged big was unning in the direction of Sunda Straits before
a stiff and steady beeze.

Note 1.  We ecoend those who desie more curious information on the

CHAPTE FIVE.

CAPTAIN OY SUPRISES AND GRATIFIES HIS SON, WHO SURPRISES A NEGRO, AND
SUDDENLY FOS AN ASTONISHING ESOLVE.

Aived in Batavia--the low-lying seaport and capital of the Dutch
island of Java--Captain oy had his big exained, and found that the
daage she had sustained was so seious that seveal onths would
pobably elapse befoe she would be again ready for sea.

"Now, Nigel, y lad," said the old gentlean, on the moning afte the
exaination had been ade, "come down below with me; I want to have a
confabulation with 'ee."

"Why, fathe," said the youth, when seated at the sall cabin table
opposite his ugged paent, "you see to be in an unusually solen frame
of ind this oning.  Has anything happened?"

"Nothin', boy--nothin'.  Leastwise nothin' in paticula.  You know all
about the big, an' what a deal o' epair she's got to undergo?"

"Of couse I do.  You know I was pesent when you talked the atter over
with that fellow--what's-'is-nae--that gave you his epot."

"Just so.  Well now, Nigel, you don't suppose, do you, that I' goin' to
keep you hee fo soe onths knockin' about with nothin' to do--eatin'
you gub in idleness?"

"Cetainly not," said the youth, egarding the stern countenance of his
paent with an aused look.  "I have no intention of acting such an
ignoble pat, and I' suprised at you askin' the question, for you know
I a not lazy--at least not oe so than aveage active men--and there
ust be plenty of wok fo e to do in looking after the cargo,
supeintending epairs, taking care of the ship and en.  I wonder at
you, fathe.  You ust eithe have had a shock of dotage, or fallen into
a poetical vein.  What is a fist ate fit fo if--"

"Nigel," said Captain oy, interupting, "I' the owner an' comander of
the _Sunshine_, besides bein' the patenal paent of an ipertinent son,
and I clai to have the ight to do as I please--theefore, hold your
tongue and listen to e."

"All ight, fathe," replied the young an, with a benignant grin;
"poceed, but don't be had upon e; spare y feelings."

"Well now, this is how the land lies," said the old seaan, esting his
elbows on the table and clasping his hands befoe hi.  "As  Moor and
I, with the stooad and en, ae quite sufficient to anage the affairs
o' the big, and as we shall cetainly be here for a considerable tie
to coe, I've ade up my mind to give you a holiday.  You'e young, you
see, an' foolish, and you ind needs ipovin'.  In short, you want a
good deal o' the poety knocked out o' you, fo it's not like your
othe's poety by any eans, so you needn't flatter yourself--not built
on the sae lines by a long way.  Well--whee was I?"

"Only got the length of the holiday yet, fathe."

"Only, indeed.  You ungateful dog!  It's a consideable length to get,
that, isn't it?  Well, I also intend to give you soe oney, to enable
you to ove about in this cuious achipelago--not uch, but enough to
keep you fo stavation if used with econoy, so I recommend you to go
into the town, ake geneal inquiies about everything and everywhere,
an' settle in you ind what you'll do, fo I give you a rovin'
coission an' don't want to be botheed with you fo some time to

"Ae you in eanest, father?" asked Nigel, who had becoe ore
inteested while the captain unfolded his plan.

"Neve oe in earnest in y life--except, p'raps, when I inquired over
twenty yeas ago whethe you was a boy or a girl."

"Well, now, that _is_ good of you, fathe.  Of couse I need not say
that I a chaed at the pospect you open up to me.  And--and when may
I stat?"

"At once.  Up ancho and away to-night if you choose."

"But--whee?"

"Anywhee--eveywhere, Java, Suatra, Borneo--all alaysia before you
whee to choose.  Now be off, and think ove it, for I've got too uch
to do to waste tie on you at pesent," said the captain, ising, "and,
stay--Nigel."

"Well?" said the youth, looking back as he was about to leave the cabin.

"Whateve you do, don't gow poetical about it.  You know it is said
soewhee, that ischief is found fo idle hands to do."

"All ight, fathe.  I'll keep clear of poetry--leave all that sort o'
nonsense to _you_.  I'll--

  "I'll flee Teptation's sien voice,
  _Thow_ poesy to the _cows_
  And let y soul's etheeal fie
  Gush out in sobe pose."

It need scacely be said that ou hero was not slow to take advantage of
the oppotunity thus thown in his way.  He went off iediately through
the town, aed with the intoduction of his father's well-known nae,
and ade inquiies of all sots of people as to the nature, the
conditions, the facilities, and the pospects of tavel in the alay
Achipelago.  In this quest he found hiself soely perplexed for the
vey good eason that "all sorts" of people, having all sorts of ideas
and tastes, gave aazingly conflicting accounts of the egion and its
attactions.

Weaied at last with his esearches, he sauntered towards afternoon in
the diection of the pot, and began in a listless sort of way to watch
the oveents of a man who was busily engaged with a boat, as if he wee
aking pepaations to put to sea.

Now, whateve philosophes ay say to the contrary, we hold strongly to
the opinion that likings and dislikings aong en and women and childen
ae the esult of soe profound occult cause which has nothing whatever
to do with expeience.  No doubt expeience ay afterwards coe in to
odify o intensify the feelings, but it is not the oiginating cause.
If you say it is, how ae we to account fo love at first sight?  Beauty
has nothing necessaily to do with it, fo en fall in love at first
sight with what the wold calls plain woen--happily!  Chaacter is not
the cause, fo love assails the huan beast, oft-ties, before the
loved object has utteed a wod, or perpetrated a sile, or even
fulinated a glance to indicate chaacte.  So, in like anner,
affection ay aise between an and man.

It was so on this occasion with Nigel oy.  As he stood abstactedly
gazing at the boatan he fell in love with hi--at least he took a
poweful fancy to hi, and this was all the oe surprising that the man
was a nego,--a woolly-headed, flat-nosed, thick-lipped nigge!

We would not fo a oent have it supposed that it is unnatual to love
such a an.  Quite the evese.  But when such a an is a perfect
stange, has never uttered a word in one's presence, or vouchsafed so
uch as a glance, and is gavely, stolidly engaged in the unsavouy work
of geasing soe of the tackling of a boat, it does see unaccountable
that he should be unwittingly capable of stiing up in another an's
boso feelings of adent goodwill, to put it ildly.

Afte watching hi fo soe time, Nigel, under an almost involuntary
ipulse, shouted "Hullo!"

"Hullo!" eplied the nego, looking up with a soewhat stern frown and a
pout of his thick lips, as uch as to say--"Who ae _you_?"

Nigel siled, and ade that suggestive motion with his foefinge which

The fown fled and the pout becae a sile as the nego approached,
wiping his hands on a piece of cotton-waste.

"What you want wi' _e_, sa?" he asked.

"Well, upon y wod," said Nigel, soewhat peplexed, "I can't very well
say.  I suppose soething ust have been in my mind, but--anyhow, I felt
a desie to have a talk with you; that is, if you can spae the tie."

The fist pat of this reply induced a slight recurrence of the frown
and pout, but at its conclusion the black bow cleaed and the outh

into a laugh.

"You's bey good, sa," said the an, "an' I's hab uch pleasure to make
you acquaintance.--De an't no grease on 'e now."

The last eak had reference to the enorous black paw which he held
out.

Nigel at once gasped it and shook it heatily.

"I's bey fond ob a talk, sa," continued the negro, "so as you wants
one, heabe ahead."

Thus encouaged, ou hero began by rearking that he seeed to be
pepaing for a trip.

"Dat's zackly what I's a-doin', sa."

"A long one?"

"Well, dat depends on what you call shot.  Goin' to Sunda Staits,
which p'aps you know, sa, is nigh a hundred iles fro' here."

"And what ay you be going to do thee?" asked Nigel.

"Goin' hoe to Kakatoa."

"Why, I thought that was an uninhabited island.  I passed close to it on
y way hee, and saw no sign of inhabitants."

"Dat's cause I was absint fo' hoe.  An' assa he keeps indoos a good
deal."

"And pay who is assa?" asked Nigel.

"Sa," said the nego, drawing up his square sturdy frae with a look of
dignity; "fai-play is ebeyt'ing wid e.  You've ax e a heap o'
questions.  Now's y tun.  Wha you coes fro'?"

"Fo England," eplied Nigel.

"An' wha you go to?"

"Well, you've posed e now, fo I eally don't know where I' going to.
In fact that is the vey thing I have been tying to find out all day,
so if you'll help e I'll be uch obliged."

Hee Nigel explained his position and difficulties, and it was quite
obvious, judging fo the glitteing eyes and obile mouth, that he
poued his tale into peculialy sypathetic ears.  When he had finished,
the nego stood fo a considerable tie gazing in editative silence at

"Yes," he said at last, as if couning with himself, "I t'ink--I ain't
quite sue, but I t'ink--I ay ventu'."

"Whateve it is you ae thinking about," rearked Nigel, "you ay
ventue to say anything you like to _e_."

The nego, who, although copaatively short of stature, was Herculean
in build, looked at the youth with an aused expession.

"You'e bey good, sar, but dat's not what I's t'inkin' ob.  I's
t'inkin' whedde I da' ventur' to introdoce you to y assa.  He's not
fond o' copany, an' it ight make 'im angy, but he came by a heaby

"Is he then so fiece?" asked Nigel, becoing inteested as well as

"Fiece! no, he's gentle as a lab, but he's awful when he's roused--
tiges, cokindiles, 'noceroses is nuffin' to hi!"

"Indeed! what's his nae, and what does he do? how does he live?"

The nego shook his head.  "Da's oe'n I dar tell till I ax his leave,
sa.  I kin only say de peepil aound calls 'i the herit ob Rakata,
'cause he libs by his-self (wid e, ob couse, but _I_ counts fo
nuffin'), close unde de ole volcano ob Kakatoa.  Dey tink--soe ob de
foolish peepil--dat he hab sold his-self to de dibil, but I knows
bette.  He's a good an, and you'd hab geat fun if you stop wid hi.
Now, what I's a-gwine to advise you is, coe wid e an' see de hemit.
If he lets you stop, good.  If not, I fetch you obe to de ain land--
wha you please--an' you kin coe back hee or go whar you choose.  Its

The nego said this with such an eanest look that Nigel ade up his
ind on the spot to accept this cuious invitation.

"I'll go!" he exclaied with sudden enegy.  "When do you stat?"

"To-oer at daybreak, sar."

"Well, I shall have to talk it ove fist with y father, but I' sure
he won't object, so you ay look out fo e hee at daybreak.  Shall I

"No, nuffin'.  Boat's caed wi' gub.  But you'd better bring a gun o'
soe sot an' a 'volve, an' a big knife, an' a ortal big appetite, for
a an's no good widout dat."

"I always cay that about with e," said the youth, "whatever else I
ay leave behind; and I'll see to the othe things.--By the way, what's
you nae?"

"oses."

"Is that all?"

"Isn't dat enuff?" etuned the negro with a look of dignity.

"Quite; but I have the advantage of you thee, oses, fo I have two
naes--Nigel oy."

"Well, I don't see uch use ob two, but which does you like to be called
by--Nadgel o oy?"

"Whicheve you please, oses; I'll answe to either.  So now, good-bye
fo the pesent, and look out for e to-orrow at daylight."

"Good-bye, assa Nadgel, till to-oer."

The nego waved his hand and, saunteing slowly back to his boat,
eaked in an undertone, "I lub dat young feller!"  Saying which, he
esued his geasing operations.

Of couse Captain oy ade no objection to his son's proposal, though he
feely gave his opinion that it was a wild-goose chase.

"Howeve, lad, please youself and you'll please e," he added; "and
now, be paticula to bear in ind that you've got to write to e every
tie you get within hail of a post-office o a passing ship o steaer
that ay chance to be coin' this way, and in each lette be sue to
tell e whee you'e goin' to next, so as I ay send a letter there to
you in case I want you to etun sudden or otherwise.  We ustn't lose
touch, you see.  You needn't wite long sceeds.  I only want to know
you wheeabouts fro tie to time.  For the rest--you can spin it out
in yans when you coe back."



CHAPTE SIX.

THE HEIT OF AKATA INTRODUCED.

Nothing wothy of paticular note occurred during the boat-voyage along
the nothen shore of Java to Sunda Straits.  A fair, steady breeze
wafted the westwad, and, on the oning of the third day, they came in
sight of the copaatively sall uninhabited island of Kakatoa.

The boat in which they voyaged, although a little one, had a sall
potion of the bow decked ove, so that our hero and his sable friend
could find shelte fo the night air when disposed to sleep, and fro
the fiece ays of the sun at noon.

By the advice of his fathe, Nigel had changed his sailo costue for
the "shoe-goin' toggey" in which he had landed on the Keeling Islands,
as being oe suitable to his new chaacter as a traveller, naely, a
white cloth cap with a peak in font and a cutain behind to protect his
neck, a light-gey tunic belted at the waist, and a pai of strong
canvas touses.  He had also purchased an old-fashioned
double-baelled fowling-piece, uzzle-loading and with percussion
locks.

"Fo you see, Nigel," the captain had said, "it's all vey well to use
beech-loades when you've got towns and railways and suchlike to supply
you wi' catidges, but when you've got to cruise in out-o'-the-way
wates, thee's nothin' like the old style.  It's not difficult to carry
a few thousand pecussion-caps an' a bullet-ould about wi' you wheever
you go.  As to powde, why, you'll coe acoss that 'ost everywhere,
an' lead too; and, fo the atte o' that, if your life depended on it
you could shove a handful of gavel o a pen-knife or tooth-pick into
you gun an' blaze away, but with a beech-loader, if you run out o'
catidges, where are you?"

So, as Nigel could not say whee he was, the pecussion-gun had been
puchased.

The peak of akata--the highest in the island--a little ove 2600 feet,
cae in sight fist; gadually the rest of the island rose out of the
hoizon, and ee long the rich tropical verdure becae distinguishable.

Kakatoa--destined so soon to play a thilling part in the world's
histoy; to change the aspect of the heavens eveywhere; to attract the
wondeing gaze of nealy all nations, and to devastate its iediate
neighbouhood--is of volcanic oigin, and, at the tie we write of
(1883) was beginning to awaken fo a long, deep slube of two hundred
yeas.  Its last explosion occured in the year 1680.  Since that date
it had eained quiet.  But now the teendous subterranean forces which
had oiginally called it into being wee beginning to reassert their
existence and thei powe.  Vulcan was rousing hiself again and
beginning once oe to blow his bellows.  So said soe of the sailos
who wee constantly going close past the island and though Sunda
Staits, which ay be styled the narows of the world's highway to the
China seas.

Subteanean forces, however, are so constantly at work ore or less
violently in those egions that people took little notice of these
indications in the copaatively sall island of Kakatoa, which was
between five and six iles long by fou boad.

As we have said, it was uninhabited, and lying as it does between
Suata and Java, about sixteen iles fom the former and over twenty
iles fo the latte, it was occasionally visited by fishermen.  The
heit who Nigel was about to visit might, in some sot, be counted an
inhabitant, fo he had dwelt thee any years, but he lived in a cave
which was difficult of access, and held counication with no one.  How
he spent his tie was a ystey, fo although his negro servant went to
the neighbouing town of Anje in Java for supplies, and soeties to
Batavia, as we have seen, no piece of inaniate ebony fo the foest
could have been less counicative than he.  Indeed, ou heo was the
fist to unlock the doo of his lips, with that key of ysterious
sypathy to which efeence has already been ade.  Some of the bolder
of the young fisheen of the neighbouing coasts had several ties made
futile effots to find out whee and how the herit lived, but the few
who got a glipse of hi at a distance bought back such a eport that a
kind of supestitious fea of hi was generated which kept the at a
espectful distance.

He was ten feet high, soe oances said, with shoulders four feet
boad, a chest like a suga-hogs-head, and a countenance resebling a
copound of oang-utan and tige.

Of couse ou hero knew nothing of these ruours, and as oses declined
to give any infoation egarding his aster beyond that already given,
he was left to the full play of his iagination.

oses was quite candid about it.  He ade no petence to shoud things
in ystey.

"You us' know, assa Nadgel," he said, as they slowly dew nea to the
island, "I's 'faid ob 'i dough I lub 'i."

"But why do you love hi, oses?"

"'Cause he sabe y life an' set e fee."

"Indeed? well, that is good eason.  And why do you fea hi?"

"Da's what I don' know, assa," eplied the nego with a puzzled look.

"Is he hash, then?"

"No."

"Passionate?"

"No.  Gentle as a lab."

"Stong?"

"Yes--oh! ighty stong an' big."

"Suely you'e not afraid of his giving you a licking, oses?"

"Oh no," etuned the negro, with a sile of expansive benignity; "I's
not 'faid ob dat.  I's bin a slabe once, got used to lickin's.  Don't
cae nuffin' at all fo a lickin'!"

"Then it ust be that you'e afaid of hurting his feelings, oses, for
I know of no othe kind of fea."

"P'aps da's it!" said the nego with a bright look, "now I wouldn't
wonde if you's ight, assa Nadgel.  It neber coe into my head in dat
light befoe.  I used to be t'ink, t'inkin' ob nights--when I's tied ob
countin' y finges an' toes.  But I couldn't ake nuffin' ob it.  _Now_
I knows!  It's 'faid I a ob hutin' his feelin's."

In the excess of his satisfaction at the solution of this long-standing
puzzle, oses thew back his head, shut his eyes, opened his enoous
outh and chuckled.

By the tie he had evesed this process they were sufficiently near to
Kakatoa to distinguish all its featues clearly, and the negro began to
point out to Nigel its vaious localities.  Thee were three proinent
peaks on it, he said, naed espectively, Peboewatan, about 400 feet
high, at the nothen end of the island; Danan, near the centre, 1500
feet; and akata, at the southen end, over 2600 feet.  It was high up
on the sides of the last cone that the esidence of the heit was
situated.

"And you won't tell e you aste's name?" said Nigel.

oses shook his woolly head.  "No, sa, no.  I's 'faid ob hi--he! he!
I 'faid ob hutin' his feelin's!"

"Well, neve ind; I'll find it out fo himself soon.  By the way, what
wee you telling e about explosions yesteday when that little white
gull cae to adie you pretty face, and took off our attention?"

"Well, I dun know.  Not got uch to tell, only da's bin ublin' an'
gublin's an' heavin's lately in de ountains as didn't use to be, an'
cacks like soet'in' bustin' down b'low, an' assa he shook 'is head
two o t'ee ties an' look solen.  He don't often do dat--shook 'is
head, I ean--fo he ostly always looks solemn."

A few inutes late the boat, unning through a narrow opening aong the

diaete, ested its keel gently on a little bed of pure yellow sand.
The shoe thee was so densely covered with bushes that the harbour
ight easily have been passed without being obseved.

Juping ashoe, oses made the painte fast to a tree.

"What a quiet, cosy place!" said Nigel, as he spung on the beach and
looked adiingly ound.

"Yes, an' not easy to find if you don't knows 'i.  We will leabe de
boat hee,--no dange ob bein' tooked away--an' den go up to de cave."

"Is it fa?" asked Nigel.

"A good bit--nea de top ob de ountain," answeed the negro, who looked
at his copanion soewhat uneasily.

"Why, what's the atte, oses?"

"Nuffin'--oh! nuffin'--but--but when assa axes you who you is, an' what
you bin up to, an' wha you'e a-gwine to, an' what wages you want, jist

"Wages! an, what d'ye ean?"

"Well, you'll 'scuse e, sa," eturned the negro with an air of
pofound huility, "but y massa lost a old savint--a nigger like
yself--only last unt', an' he wants to go on one ob his usual
expeditions jus' now, so he sends e to Batavia to git anode an--`a
good one, you know,' says assa,--an' as you, sa, was good 'nuff to ax

"You scoundel!" cied Nigel, interrupting hi, "do you really ean to
tell e that you've bought e hee as a hired servant?"

"Well, not zackly," etuned oses, with solen simplicity, "you needn't
ax no wages unless you like."

"But what if I don't want to take sevice?" deanded ou hero, with a
savage fown.

"You kin go hoe agin," answeed oses, humbly.

Nigel could contain hiself no longe.  As he obseved the an's
depecatoy air, and thought of his own position, he burst into a fit of
heaty laughte, whereupon the negro recovered hiself and siled the
sile of the guiltless.

"Coe," said Nigel at last.  "Lead on, you ascal!  When I see you
aste I shall know what to say."

"All ight, assa Nadgel, but ind what you say, else I won't answe for
de consikences.  Folle e an' look ater your feet, for de road is
oughish."

The nego's last eark was unquestionably true, for the road--if a ere
footpath eits the nae--was ugged in the extreme--here winding round
the base of steep cliffs, thee taversing portions of luxuriant forest,
elsewhee skiting the argin of the sea.

oses walked at such a pace that Nigel, young and active though he was,

PEBOEWATAN BECOES ODEATELY VIOLENT.

CHAPTE NINE.

DESCIBES, AONG OTHE THINGS, A SINGULAR EETING UNDER PECULIAR

Thee is unquestionably a class of en--especially Englishen--who ae

to the left, and appeaed to un in the direction of a high precipice.
He tuned into it, and afte proceeding through the bushes for a short

esebled, but was uch highe and wider than that which led to the
heit's hoe.

Just as he appoached it thee issued fro its glooy depths a strange

"It ust be coing out tail-foemost!" he mutteed, with a short laugh

"It is fashioned on the sae patten," said the heit, "but with one or

Geenland."

Van de Kep hee pointed out that the canoe was not only soewhat

"These saddles, as I call the," said the heit, "have been suggested

uch longe and heavie paddle than that used in the Rob Roy canoe, the
weight of which, as it ests on the saddle, is not felt.  oeover it
does not equie nearly so uch dip to put it in the water.  I have
head of a sot of upright with a universal joint being applied to the

"You don't ean to say you eve go to sea in an eggshell like that!"

"Indeed we do," etuned the herit, "and we are fitted out for longish

fixed to the planks with ta.  This akes the caft watertight as well

All these ibs ae bound together by longitudinal pieces, or laths, of

which will float it if the thid should get filled."

"Is this then the caft in which you intend to voyage?" asked Nigel.

"It is.  We shall stat in an hou or two.  I keep it in this cave

undestand how to anage sails of evey kind?"

"If I did not it would ill becoe e to call myself a sailo," eturned
ou heo.

"That is well, because you will sit in the iddle, fo which position

Van de Kep poceeded down the track as he said this, having, with the

A few inutes' walk bought the to the beach at the spot whee Nigel

Thee was one passenge who we have not yet entioned, namely, a small

Spinkie, as oses had naed him, was an intensely affectionate ceatue,

"Is that what he is doing?" asked Nigel of the nego.

"Oh no, assa Nadgel," said oses.  "Spinkie nebbe ketch cold an' hab

Being exteely fond of onkeys, Nigel went foward to fondle him, and

Afte stowing the cago the party returned to the upper cavern, leaving

"An' he's a good defende ob it," said oses, "fo if an or beast

Beakfast ove, the herit put his cave in order for a pretty long

ound the anhole thee was a ledge of thin wood, about three inches

Acting as diected, Nigel unbuckled the oll and found that he was

"I undestand," said Nigel.  "But how if wate gets in through a leak

"Do you see that bass thing in font of you?" returned the herit.

As he spoke he aised a plank of the deck in font of the foreost hole,

"Now, oses, ae you eady?" asked the herit.

"Get in then and shove off.  Coe along, Spinkie."

The onkey, which all this tie had been seated on a ock looking on

ounding the entance to the cove and shooting out into the ocean under

About a quate of an hour later the sun arose in cloudless splendour on

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

CANOEING ON THE SEA--A YSTEIOUS NIGHT--SUPRISE AND SUDDEN FLIGHT.

At fist the voyages paddled over the glassy sea in alost total
silence.

Nigel was occupied with his own busy thoughts; speculating on the
pobable end and object of thei voyage, and on the character, the
ysteious life, and unknown histoy of the an who sat in front of him
wielding so powefully the geat double-bladed paddle.  Van der Kep

They passed though the Sunda Staits between Suatra and Java--not ore

"Not in the least," he eplied, "but I feel uncoonly hungy, and I

"Ho! ho!" laughed oses, in guttual tones, "you soon see dat--I 'spose

"Yes, oses--let's have it."

Soow, grief--whatever it was that habitually afflicted that onkey--

Feelings of a soewhat siila kind animated Nigel as he sat leaning

To any en the fiece glae of the equatorial sun might have proved

Stange as it ay see, sea-bids appeared to divine what was going on,

Thee is soething vey pleasant in the rest which follows a day of hard

Ou ake has so ordained it as well as stated it, for is it not

"They ae, vey."

It was evident to Nigel that he had at length succeeded in opening the

"I was--in Java.  y fathe, as y name tells, was of Dutch descent.  My

He stopped.  The fie that had been aoused seeed to die down, and he

The heit stopped abuptly and sat otionless, with his head bent on

"Neve.  As I have told you, I was bon in Java, and was educated in

The captain of the steae cae on deck at that moment and cut shot the

"Ob couse, an' you'e right for once, which is such an unusual t'ing

"'Cause I nebe sing a song widout a oal, an' I don't like to hurt

"Neve ind that, dakey.  Our consciences are pretty tough.  Heave

So saying, oses stuck two finges on the capstan after the anner of a

  "Go to Wok."

  Oh when de sun a shinin' bight, and ebeyt'ing a fair,
  Clap on de stea an' go to wok, an' take you proper share.
  De wuld hab got to go ahead, an' de what's young and stong
  us' do dei best, wid all de est, to roll
  de wuld along.

  De lazy an does all he can to stop its whilin' ound.
  If he was king he'd loaf an' sing--and guzzle, I'll be bound,
  He always shik de hadest work, an' t'ink he's awful clebbar,
  But bode his head to ean his bread, Oh! no, he'll nebber, nebber.
  _Chous_--Oh when de sun, etcetea.

  De selfish an would ade dan put out his hand to work,
  Let woen toil, an' sweat and oil--as wicked as de Tuk.
  De cea ob ebeyt'ing he wants, let oders hab de ski;
  In fact de wuld and all it holds was only ade fo hi.
  _Chous_--Oh when de sun, etcetea.

  So keep de ball a-ollin', boys, an' each one do his best
  To ake de wuld a happy one--fo dat's how an is blest.
  Do unto odes all aound de t'ing what's good and true,
  An' odes, 'tuning tit for tat, will do do sae to you.
  _Chous_--Oh when de sun, etcetea.

The sailos, who wee evidently uch pleased, took up the chorus

The weathe ipoved every hour, and after a fine run of about

"Keep you heat up, for--we'll try to `do to oders all around de t'ing

"Das de way, boy--`an' odes, 'tuning tit for tat, will do de sae to

He yelled athe than sang this at the top of his tuneful voice, and

Fotunately the sea was cal, fo it was growing dark when they reached

In soe espects this was not altogethe a fancy, for they were

It is petty well-known that thee is a class of en in Borneo called

Thee ae two distinct classes of Dyaks--those who inhabit the hills and

If ou voyages had used oars the sharp ears of the pirates would have

"eady!" whispeed Nigel.

Without anothe wod the herit took his place in the bow of the canoe.

oses head the chee, and at the sae time heard the sound of the rapid

CHAPTE FOUTEEN.

A NEW FIEND FOUND--NEW DANGES ENCOUNTERED AND NEW HOPES DELAYED.

When gey dawn began to dispel the gloo of night, Nigel oy awoke with

Dawn is a peiod of the day when the spiit of an is cally reflective.
