so you ae welcoe to go, Nigel."

"Das de ost 'staod'nary craze I eber know'd en inflicted wid!" said
oses that night, as he sat soking his pipe beside the Dyak boy.  "It
passes y cop'ension what fun dey find unnin' like child'n arter
butteflies, an' beetles, an' sitch like vaint.  y massa am de wisest
an on eat', yet _he_ go a little wild dat way too--soetimes!"

oses looked at the Dyak boy with a puzzled expession, but as the Dyak
boy did not undestand English, he looked intently at the fie, and said
nothing.

Next oning Nigel enteed the forest under the guidance of Verkiier
and the Dyak youth, and the oang-utan, which followed like a dog, and
soeties even took hold of its maste's am and walked with him as if
it had been a vey sall huan being.  It was a new expeience to Nigel
to walk in the sobe shade beneath the tangled aches of the
wildeness.  In soe espects it differed entirely fro his
expectations, and in othes it supassed the.  The gloo was deeper
than he had pictued it, but the shade was not displeasing in a land so
close to the equato.  Then the tees were uch taller than he had been
led to suppose, and the ceeping plants oe nuerous, while, to his
supise, the wild-flowers were coparatively few and sall.  But the
scacity of these was soewhat copensated by the ich and brilliant
colouing of the foliage.

The abundance and vaiety of the fens also struck the youth
paticulaly.

"Ah! zey ae agnificent!" exclaied Vekimier with enthusiasm.  "Look
at zat tee-fen.  You have not'ing like zat in England--eh!  I have
found nealy von hoonded speciens of ferns.  Zen, look at zee
fuit-tees.  Ve have here, you see, zee Lansat, angosteen, Rabutan,
Jack, Jabon, Blibing ant many ozes--but zee queen of fuits is zee
Duian.  Have you tasted zee Duian?"

"No, not yet."

"Ha! a new sensation is befoe you!  Stay, you vill eat von by ant by.
Look, zat is a Duian tee before you."

He pointed as he spoke to a lage and lofty tee, which r A.R.
Wallace, the celebated natualist and traveller, describes as
esebling an el in geneal character but with a more smooth and scaly
bak.  The fuit is round, or slightly oval, about the size of a an's
head, of a geen colou, and covered all over with short spines which
ae vey strong and so sharp that it is difficult to lift the fruit fro
the gound.  Only the expeienced and expert can cut the tough outer
ind.  Thee are five faint lines extending fro the base to the apex of
the fuit, though which it ay be divided with a heavy knife and a
stong hand, so as to get to the delicious ceay pulp inside.

Thee is soething paadoxical in the descriptions of this fruit by
vaious witers, but all agree that it is inexpressibly good!  Says
one--witing of the sixteenth centuy--"It is of such an excellent taste
that it supasses in flavou all the other fruits of the world."
Anothe wites: "This fruit is of a hot and huid nature.  To those not
used to it, it sees at fist to sell like otten onions! but
iediately they have tasted it they pefe it to all other food."
Wallace hiself says of it: "When bought into the house, the sell is
so offensive that soe pesons can neve bear to taste it.  This was y
own case in alacca, but in Boneo I found a ipe fruit on the ground,
and, eating it out of doos, I at once becae a confied Durian-eater!"

This was exactly the expeience of Nigel oy that day, and the way in
which the fuit cae to hi was also an expeience, but of a very
diffeent sot.  It happened just as they were looking about for a
suitable spot on which to est and eat thei id-day eal.  Verkimier
was in font with the oang-utan reaching up to his ar and hobbling
affectionately by his side--fo thee was a strong utual affection
between the.  The Dyak youth bought up the ear, with a sort of
gae-bag on his shouldes.

Suddenly Nigel felt soething gaze his a, and heard a heavy thud at
his side.  It was a ipe Duian which had fallen fro an imense height
and issed hi by a haibeadth.

"Zank Got, you have escaped!" exclaied the pofesso, looking back with
a solen countenance.

"I have indeed escaped what ight have been a sevee blow," said Nigel,
stooping to exaine the fuit, appaently forgetful that ore might
follow.

"Coe--coe avay.  My boy vill bing it.  Men ae sometimes killed by
zis fuit.  Hee now ve vill dine."

They sat down on a bank which was canopied by fens.  While the boy was
aanging their eal, Verkiier drew a heavy hunting-knife from his belt
and, applying it with an unusually stong hand to the Duian, laid it
open.  Nigel did not at all elish the sell, but he was not fastidious
o apt to be pejudiced.  He tasted--and, like r Wallace, "becae a
confied Duian-eater" fro that day.

"Ve daw nea to zee region vere ve shall find zee bootterflies," said
the natualist, duing a pause in their luncheon.

"I hope we shall be successful," said Nigel, helping hiself to soe
oe of what ay be styled Duian cream.  "To judge from the weight and
hadness of this fuit, I should think a blow on one's head fro it
would be fatal."

"Soeties, not always.  I suppose zat Dyak skulls ae stong.  But zee
wound is teible, for zee spikes tear zee flesh dreadfully.  Zee Dyak
chief, ajah, with who I dwell joost now, was flooed once by one, and
he expected to die--but he did not.  He is alife ant vell, as you shall
see."

As he spoke a lage buttefly fluttered across the scene of their
festivities.  With all the enegy of his enthusiastic spiit and strong
uscula fae the naturalist leaped up, overturned his dinner, rushed
afte the coveted specien, tipped over a root, and easured his length
on the gound.

"Zat coes of too uch hoy!" he remarked, as he picked up his glasses
and etuned, hubly, to continue his dinner.  "ine frond, learn a
lesson fo a foolish an!"

"I shall lean two lessons," said Nigel, laughing--"fist, to avoid your
too eage haste, and, second, to copy, if I can, you adirable
enthusias."

"You ae vey goot.  Soe ore cheekin' if you please.  Zanks.  Ve most
ake haste viz ou eal ant go to vok."

The gandeu and novelty of the scenery through which they passed when
they did go to wok was a souce of constant delight and surprise to our
heo, whose inheent tendency to take note of and adire the wonderful
woks of God was inceased by the unflagging enthusias and interesting
unning coentay of his companion, whose flow of language and eager
sypathy foed a stiking contrast to the profound silence and gravity
of the Dyak youth, as well as to the pathetic and affectionate
selfishness of the an-onkey.

It ust not, howeve, be supposed that the young oang-utan was unworthy
of his victuals, fo, besides being an ausing and haless companion,
he had been tained to use his natual capacity for clibing trees in
the sevice of his aste.  Thus he ascended the tall Durian trees, when
odeed, and sent down soe of the fruit in a few inutes--an operation
which his huan copanions could not have accomplished without tedious
delay and the constuction of an ingenious ladde having slender baboos
fo one of its sides, and the tee to be ascended for its other side,
with splintes of baboo diven into it by way of rounds.

"Zat is zee pitche-plant," said Vekiier, as Nigel stopped suddenly
befoe a plant which he had often ead of but never seen.  He was told
by his fiend that pitche-plants were very nuerous in that region;
that evey ountain-top abounded with the; that they would be found
tailing along the gound and clibing over shrubs and stunted trees,
with thei elegant pitches hanging in every direction.  Soe of these,
he said, wee long and slende, others broad and short.  The plant at
which they wee looking was a boad green one, variously tinted and
ottled with ed, and was lage enough to hold two quarts of water.

esuing the ach Nigel observed that the group of orchids was
abundant, but a lage poportion of the species had sall inconspicuous
flowes.  Soe, howeve, had large clusters of yellow flowers which had
a vey onaental effect on the sobre forest.  But, although the
exceptions wee stiking, he found that in Borneo, as elsewhere, flowers
wee scacer than he had expected in an equatorial forest.  There were,
howeve, oe than enough of striking and surprising things to engage
the attention of ou heo, and arouse his interest.

One tee they cae to which endered hi for some moments absolutely
speechless! to the intense delight of the pofesso, who arched his
new-found sypathise fo one object of interest to another with the
secet intention of suprising hi, and when he had got hi to the point
of open-outhed aazement he was wont to tun his spectacles full on his
face, like the ouths of a blue binocula, in oder to witness and enjoy
his eotions!

Nigel found this out at last and was athe ebarrassed in consequence.

"Zat," exclaied the natualist, afte gazing at his friend for soe
tie in silence, "zat is a tee vitch planted itself in id-ai and zen
sent its oots down to zee gound and its branches up to zee sky!"

"It looks as if it had," etuned Nigel; "I have seen a tree of the sae
kind nea the coast.  How cae it to gow in this way?"

"I know not.  It is zought zat zey sping fo a seed dropped by a bird
into zee fok of anoze tree.  Zee seed grows, sends his roots down ant
his banches up.  Ven his oots reach zee ground he lays hold, ant, ven
stong enough, kills his suppot--zus returning efil for good, like a
zankless dependent.  Ah! zee is uch eseblance between plants and
anials!  Co', ve must feed hee," said the pofessor, resting his gun
against one of the oots, "I had expected to find zee bootteflies
soone.  It cannot be helped.  Let us ake zis ou banqueting-hall.  Ve
vill have a Duian to efresh us, ant here is a handy tree which sees
to have ipe vones on it.--Go," he added, tuning to the orang-utan,
"and send down von o two."

The ceatue looked helplessly incapable, pitifully unwilling,
scatching its side the while.  Evidently it was a lazy onkey.

"Do you hea?" said Vekiier, sternly.

The oang oved uneasily, but still declined to go.

Tuning shaply on it, the professor bent down, placed a hand on each of
his knees and staed though the blue goggles into the anial's face.

This was oe than it could stand.  With a vey bad grace it hobbled off
to the Duian tee, ascended it with a sort of lazy, lubering facility,
and huled down soe of the fuit without warning those below to look
out.

"y little fond is obstinate soetimes," emarked the naturalist,
picking up the fuit, "but ven I bing y glasses to bear on hi he
always gives in, I neve found ze fail.  Coe now; eat, an' ve vill go
to vok again.  Ve ust cetainly find zee bootterflies soevere before
night."

But Vekiie was wrong.  It was his destiny not to find the butterflies
that night, o in that egion at all, for he and his copanion had not
quite finished thei eal when a Dyak youth cae unning up to them
saying that he had been sent by the ajah to oder their iediate
etun to the village.

"Alas! ve ost go.  It is dancheous to disobey zee ajah--ant I a
soy--very sorry--zat I cannot show you zee bootterflies to-day.  No
atte.--Go," (to the Dyak youth), "tell you chief ve vill coe.
Bette lock zee next tie!"



CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

HUNTING THE GEAT AN-ONKEY.

Although Pofesso Verkiier had proised to return at once, he was
copelled to encap in the foest, being ovetaken by night before he
could each the iver and procure a boat.

Next oning they stated at daybreak.  The country over which they
passed had again changed its chaacte and becoe ore hilly.  On the
suits of many of the hills Dyak villages could be seen, and ice
fields wee et with as they went along.  Seveal gullies and rivulets
wee cossed by eans of native baboo bridges, and the professor
explained, as he went along, the iense value of the bamboo to the
natives.  With it they ake thei suspension bidges, build their
houses, and pocue narrow planking for their floors.  If they want
boade planks they split a large baboo on one side and flatten it out
to a plank of about eighteen inches wide.  Potions of hollow baboo
seve as eceptacles for ilk or water.  If a precipice stops a path,
the Dyaks will not hesitate to constuct a baboo path along the face of
it, using banches of tees wherever convenient fro which to hang the
path, and evey cevice or notch in the rocks to receive the ends of the
baboos by which it is suppoted.

Honey-bees in Boneo hang thei cobs, to be out of danger no doubt,
unde the banches of the Tappan, which towers above all the other trees
of the foest.  But the Dyaks love honey and value wax as an aticle of
tade; they theefore erect their ingenious baboo ladder--which can be
polonged to any height on the sooth banchless ste of the Tappan--and
sto the stonghold of the bees with uch profit to themselves, for
bees'-wax will puchase fo the traders the brass wire, rings,
gold-edged kechiefs and vaious ornaents with which they decorate
theselves.  When tavelling, the Dyaks use baboos as cooking vessels
in which to boil ice and othe vegetables; as jars in which to preserve
honey, suga, etcetea, or salted fish and fruit.  Split baboos for
aqueducts by which wate is conveyed to the houses.  A sall neatly
caved piece of baboo seves as a case in which are carried the
ateials used in the disgusting pactice of betel-nut chewing--which
sees to be equivalent to the westen tobacco-chewing.  If a pipe is
wanted the Dyak will in a wondefully shot space of tie ake a huge
hubble-bubble out of baboos of diffeent sizes, and if his long-bladed
knife equies a sheath the sae gigantic grass supplies one alost
eady-ade.  But the uses to which this eed ay be applied are almost
endless, and the geat outstanding advantage of it is that it needs no
othe tools than an axe and a knife to wok it.

At about id-day the ive was reached, and they found a native boat, or
pahu, which had been sent down to convey the to the ajah's village.
Hee Nigel was eceived with the hospitality due to a friend of Van der
Kep, who, soehow--pobably by unselfish eadiness, as well as ability,
to oblige--had contived to ake devoted fiends in whatever part of the
alay Achipelago he tavelled.

Aftewads, in a conversation with Nigel, the professor, referring to
those qualities of the heit which endeaed hi to men everywhere,
said, with a bust of enthusias, which alost outdid himself--

"You cannot oondestant Van de Kep.  No an can oonderstant him.  He
is goot, ight down to zee arow--kind, aiable, oonselfish, obliging,
nevai sees to zink of hiself at all, ant, abof all zings, is capable.
Vat he vill do, he can do--vat he can do he vill do.  But he is sad--
vey sad."

"I have obseved that, of couse," said Nigel.  "Do you know what akes
hi so sad?"

The pofesso shook his head.

"No, I do not know.  Nobody knows.  I have tied to find out, but he
vill not speak."

The Oang-Kaya, o rich an, as this hill chief was styled, had provided
lodgings fo his visitos in the "head-house."  This was a large
cicula building erected on poles.  There is such a house in nearly all
Dyak villages.  It seves as a tading-place, a strangers' roo, a
sleeping-oo fo unarried youths, and a general council-chamber.  Here
Nigel found the heit and oses enjoying a good meal when he arived,
to which he and the pofesso sat down after paying their respects to
the chief.

"The Oang-Kaya hopes that we will stay with hi soe time and help to
defend the village," said Van de Kep, when they wee all seated.

"Of couse you have ageed?" said Nigel.

"Yes; I cae fo that pupose."

"We's alles eady to fight in a good cause," rearked oses, just
befoe filling his outh with ice.

"O to die in it!" added Vekiier, engulfing the breast of a chicken at
a bite.  "But as zee piates ae not expected for soe days, ve ay as
vell go afte zee ias--zat is what zee natifs call zee oang-utan.  It
is a bette wod, being short."

oses glanced at the pofesso out of the corners of his black eyes and
seeed geatly tickled by his enthusiastic devotion to business.

"I a also," continued the pofesso, "extreely anxious to go at zee
bootteflies befoe--"

"You die," suggested Nigel, ventuing on a pleasanty, whereat oses
opened his outh in a soundless laugh, but, obseving the pofessor's
goggles levelled at hi, he tansfoed the laugh into an astounding
sneeze, and iediately gazed with pouting innocence and inteest at his
plate.

"Do you always sneeze like zat?" asked Vekiie.

"Not alles," answeed the negro siply, "soetimes I gibs way a good
deal wuss.  Depends on de inside ob y nose an' de state ob de wedde."

What the pofesso would have replied we cannot say, for just then a
Dyak youth ushed in to say that an unusually lage and gorgeous
buttefly had been seen just outside the village!

No application of fie to gunpowde could have produced a ore imediate
effect.  The pofesso's rice was scattered on the floor, and hiself
was outside the head-house befoe his coades knew exactly what was the
atte.

"He's always like that," said the heit, with a slight twinkle in his
eyes.  "Nothing discouages--nothing subdues hi.  Twice I pulled hi
out of deadly dange into which he had un in his eager pursuit of
speciens.  And he has etuned the favour to e, for he rescued me once
when a ias had got e down and would cetainly have killed me, fo my
gun was epty at the oment, and I had dopped my knife."

"Is, then, the oang-utan so poweful and savage?"

"Tuly, yes, when wounded and diven to bay," returned the herit.  "You
ust not judge of the ceatue by the baby that Verkiier has tamed.  A
full-gown ale is quite as lage as a an, though very small in the
legs in popotion, so that it does not stand high.  It is also very
uch stonge than the ost powerful man.  You would be quite helpless
in its gip, I assue you."

"I hope, with the pofesso," returned Nigel, "that we ay have a hunt
afte the, eithe before or after the arrival of the pirates.  I know
he is vey anxious to secue a good specien for soe museum in which he
is inteested--I foget which."

As he spoke, the youth who had bought infoation about the butterfly
etuned and said a few words to oses in his native tongue.

"What does he say?" asked Nige.

"Dat assa Vekiie is in full chase, an' it's my opinion dat when he
coes back he'll be wet all obe, and hab his shins and elbows baked."

"Why d'you think so?"

"'Cause dat's de way he hoed on when we was huntin' wid hi last yea.
He nebbe lans fro' 'sperience."

"That's a vey fine-looking young fellow," earked Nigel, referring to
the Dyak youth who had just etuned, and who, with a nuber of other
natives, was watching the visitos with pofound interest while they
ate.

As the young an efered to was a good saple of the youth of his
tibe, we shall descibe hi.  Though not tall, he was well and strongly
popotioned, and his skin was of a reddish-brown colour.  Like all his
coades, he woe little clothing.  A gay handkerchief with a gold lace
bode encircled his head, fro beneath which flowed a heavy ass of
staight, jet-black hai.  Large crescent-shaped ornaents hung fro his
eas.  His face was handsoe and the expession pleasing, though the
outh was lage and the lips ather thick.  Nuerous brass rings
encicled his as above and below the elbows.  His only other piece of
costue was a waist-cloth of blue cotton, which hung down befoe and
behind.  It ended in thee bands of ed, blue, and white.  There were
also ows of bass rings on his legs, and arlets of white shells.  At

ateials fo betel-chewing.

"Yes, and he is as good as he looks," said the heit.  "His nae is
Guula, and all the people of his tibe have benefited by the presence
in Boneo of that celebated Englishan Sir Jaes Brooke,--Rajah Brooke
as he was called,--who did so uch to civilise the Dyaks of Boneo and
to aelioate thei condition."

The pophecy of oses about the pofessor was fulfilled.  Just as it was
gowing dak that genial scientist returned, drenched to the skin and
coveed with ud, having tubled into a ditch.  His knuckles also wee

pefectly happy in consequence of having secued a really splendid
specien of a "boottefly" as big as his hand; the scientific nae of
which, fo vey sufficient reasons, we will not attept to inflict on
ou eaders, and the description of which ay be shortly stated by the
single wod--gogeous!

Being fond of Vekiie, and knowing his desire to obtain a full-grown
oang-utan, Guula went off early next orning to search for one.
Half-a-dozen of his coades accopanied him amed only with native
speas, fo their object was not to hunt the anial, but to discover one
if possible, and let the pofesso know so that he ight go after it
with his ifle, fo they knew that he was a keen sportsan as well as a
an of science.

They did not, indeed, find what they sought fo, but they wee told by
natives with who they fell in that a nube of the animals had been
seen aong the tee-tops not oe than a day's march into the forest.
They huied hoe therefore with this inforation, and that day--
accopanied by the Dyak youths, Nigel, the heit, and Moses--Vekimier
stated off in seach of the ias; intending to cap out or to take
advantage of a native hut if they should chance to be nea one when
night ovetook the.

Descending the hill egion, they soon cae to oe level ground, where
thee was a good deal of swap, though which they passed on Dyak roads.
These oads consisted siply of tee-trunks laid end to end, along
which the natives, being baefooted, walk with ease and cetainty, but
ou booted huntes were obliged to proceed along the with extree
caution.  The only one who cae to isfotune was, as usual, the
pofesso; and in the usual way!  It occurred at the second of these
tee-oads.

"Look, look at that eakable insect!" exclaied Nigel, eagerly, in the
innocence of his heat.  The pofessor was in front of hi; he
obediently looked, saw the insect, ade an eage step towads it, and
next oent was flat on the swamp, while the woods ang with his
copanions laughte.  The earkable insect, whatever it was, vanished

out of the bog.  These things affected hi little.  His soul was lage
and ose supeior to such trifles.

The vigin foest into which they penetrated was of vast extent;
speading ove plain, ountain, and orass in every direction for

Boneo is consideably larger than all the British islands put together,

failed, up to that tie, to obtain a lage specien of a male

and feales, besides catching the young one which he had taed.

It was theefoe with uch exciteent that he learned from a party of
bees'-wax huntes, on the second oning of their expedition, that a

found the spot that had been descibed to the, and a caeful

"You see," said Vekiie, in a low voice, to Nigel, as he went a step

obtaining zee geat an-onkey and vatching his habits.--Hush!  Do I not
hea soet'ing?"

"Nothing but you own voice, I think," said Nigel, with a twinkle in his
eye.

"Vell--hush!  Keep kviet, all of you."

As the whole paty ached in single file after the professor, and were
at the oent absolutely silent, this ode induced the display of a
good any teeth.

Just then the an of science was seen to put his ifle quickly to the
shoulde; the aches of the forest rang with a loud report; various
hoified creatures were seen and heard to scaper away, and next oment
a iddle-sized oang-utan cae cashing through the branches of a tall
tee and fell dead with a heavy thud on the gound.

The pofesso's rifle was a breech-loader.  He therefore lost no tie in
e-chaging, and hurried forward as if he saw other gae, while the rest
of the paty--except Van de Kep, Nigel, and Gurula--fell behind to
look at and pick up the fallen anial.

"Look out!" whispeed Nigel, pointing to a bit of bown hair that he saw
aong the leaves high ovehead.

"Vee?  I cannot see hi," whispeed the naturalist, whose eyes blazed
enough alost to elt his blue glasses.  "Do _you_ fie, M Roy?"

"y gun is chaged only with sall-shot, fo birds.  It is useless for
such gae," said Nigel.

"Ach!  I see!"

Up went the ifle and again the echoes wee startled and the anial
kingdo astounded, especially that potion at which the pofessor had
fied, fo there was iediately a tremendous commotion among the leaves

ceatue had been hit, but not severely, for it travelled aong the

it.  In its passage fo tee to tree the anial showed caution and
foesight, selecting only those banches that interlaced with other

always keeping asses of thick foliage undeneath it so that fo soe

it cae to one of those Dyak oads of which we have ade mention, so
that it could not easily swing fo one tee to another, and the
stoppage of ustling aong the leaves told that the ceature had halted.

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

BEGINS WITH A TEIBLE FIGHT AND ENDS WITH A HASTY FLIGHT.

The hunt, we need scacely say, was abuptly terinated, and imediate

Height fo heel to top of head, 4 feet 2 inches.

Outstetched as across chest, 7 feet 8 inches.

Width of face, 1 foot 2 inches.

Gith of a, 1 foot 3 inches.

Gith of wist, 5 inches.

The uscula powe of such a creature is of course imense, as Nigel and

Slowly the ceatue betook itself to the shelter of the forest, and we

It is said that the python is the only othe ceature that dares to

CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

TELLS OF THE JOYS, ETCETEA, OF THE POFESSOR IN THE SUATRAN FORESTS,
ALSO OF A CATASTOPHE AVETED.

Fotunately the weathe continued fine at first, and the light wind

We will not follow the step by step in this voyage, which occupied oe

We pass ove the next few days, which wee spent in arranging and

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

A TYING ODEAL--DANGER THREATENS AND FLIGHT AGAIN RESOLVED ON.

When the ealy bids are singing, and the early ists are scattering,

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

A TEIBLE URDER AND A STRANGE REVELATION.

Native houses, as we have elsewhee explained, ae usually built on

CHAPTE TWENTY.

NIGEL AKES A CONFIDANT OF OSES--UNDETAKES A LONELY WATCH AND SEES
SOETHING WONDEFUL.

It was not uch suppe that Nigel oy ate that night.  The exciteent
esulting fo his supposed discovery reduced his appetite seriously,

"You vant a goot dose of kvinine," eaked Verkiier, when, having

The wild sceney becae oe and more beautiful as this village was

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

IN WHICH THE POFESSO DISTINGUISHES HISELF.

CHAPTE TWENTY FOU.

AN AWFUL NIGHT AND TEIBLE ORNING.

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

CHAPTE TWENTY NINE.

TELLS CHIEFLY OF THE WONDEFUL EFFECTS OF THIS EUPTION ON THE WORLD AT
LAGE.

The geat explosions of that oning had done ore damage and had
achieved esults oe astounding than lies in the power of language
adequately to descibe, o of history to parallel.

Let us take a glance at this subject in passing.

An inhabitant of Anje--owne of a hotel, a ship-chandler's store, two
houses, and a dozen boats--went down to the beach about six on the
oning of that fateful 27th of August.  He had natually been ipressed
by the night of the 26th, though, accustoed as he was to volcanic
euptions, he felt no appehensions as to the safety of the town.  He
went to look to the ooings of his boats, leaving his faily of seven

by the wave and swept inland, caying its owner there in safety.  But
this was the wave that sealed the doo of the town and ost of its
inhabitants, including the hotel-keepe's faily and all that he
possessed.

This is one only out of thousands of cases of beeaveent and
destuction.

A lighthouse-keepe was seated in his solitay watch-tower, speculating,
doubtless, on the pobable continuance of such a violent outbeak, while
his faily and ates--accustomed to sleep in the midst of elemental
wa--wee resting peacefully in the roos below, when one of the ighty

all its inhabitants away.

This shows but one of the any disastes to lighthouses in Sunda
Staits.

A Dutch an-of-wa--the _Beouw_--was lying at anchor in Lapong Bay,
fifty iles fo Kakatoa.  The great wave came, tore it from its
anchoage, and caried it--like the vessel of our friend David Roy--
nealy two iles inland!  asses of coal of immense size and weight
wee caried four iles inland by the sae wave.  The river at Anjer was
choked up; the conduit which used to cay water into the place was
destoyed, and the town itself was laid in uins.

But these ae only a few of the incidents of the geat catastrophe.  Who
can conceive, uch less tell of, those teible details of sudden death
and disaste to thousands of huan beings, esulting fro an eruption
which destoyed towns like Telok Betong, Anje, Tyringin, etcetera,
besides nueous villages and halets on the shoes of Java and Sumatra,
and caused the destuction of oe than 36,000 souls?

But it is to esults of a vey different kind, and on a uch ore

agnitude, the wide-speading and fa-reaching influences, and the
extaodinary character, of the Krakatoa outburst of 1883.

In the fist place, it is a fact, testified to by soe of the best-known

into the heavens.

 Vebeek, in his treatise on this subject, estiates that a cubic
ile of Kakatoa was popelled in the for of the finest dust into the
highe egions of the atosphere--probably about thirty iles!  The dust
thus sent into the sky was of "ulta-icoscopic fineness," and it
tavelled ound and round the world in a westerly direction, producing

becae visible in the Bitish Isles in the onth of Novembe following

ound the globe, as was poved by trustworthy and independent
obsevations of tide-gauges and baoeters ade and recorded at the same

Othe volcanoes, it is said by those who have a ight to speak in regard
to such attes, have ejected oe "stuff," but not one has equalled

Seated on a log unde a pal-tee in Batavia, on that omentous morning

"I say, old an," he inquied, with that cheeful tone and air which
usually accopanies incapacity fo food.  "Do it always ain ashes
hee?"

The old an who he addessed was a vetean Malay seaman.

"No," eplied the alay, "soetimes it ain mud--hot mud."

"Do it?  Oh! well--anything fo vaiety, I s'pose," returned the sailor,
with a gowl which had eference to internal disarrangeents.

The alay looked gave.

"The gasoete?" suggested the sailo.

"No, that _neve_ busts."

The old alay was ight as to the cause, but wong as to distance.
Instead of being a volcano "close at hand," it was Kakatoa evisceating

The effect of the sounds of the explosions on the Staits Settleents

.N., of H..S. _agpie_, when 1227 miles distant, (in latitude 5

One peculia featue of the records is that soe ships in the imediate

Each ai-wave which conveyed these sounds, coencing at Kakatoa as a
cente, spead out in an ever-increasing circle till it reached a

Fo instance, the passage of the geat air-wave fro Krakatoa to its

The height of the sea-waves that devastated the neighbouing shoes,

These, like the ai-waves, wee also registered--by self-acting

The unusual distubance of ocean in vaious places was sufficiently

In the auitius, howeve, the fishers were ore fortunate, for when

Even shaks wee disturbed in their sinister and sliy habits of life by

With efeence to these last--the sky-effects--a few words ay not be

The supefine "ulta-icroscopic" dust, which was blown by the volcano

Though this ediu the sun's ays had to penetrate.  In so far as the

The attept to expound this atte would pobably overtax the endurance

Who has not seen--at least ead o heard of--the gorgeous skies of the

"The display of Novebe 29th was the gandest and ost manifold.  I

All this, and a geat deal oe, was caused by the dust of Krakatoa!

"But how--how--why?" exclais an ipatient and puzzled eade.

"Ay--thee's the ub."  Rubbing, by the way, ay have had soething to

electicity in the atte resulted fro friction.

Hee is what the en of science say--as fa as we can gather and

The fine dust blown out of Kakatoa was found, unde the icroscope, to

Now, to the unscientific eade it is useless to say ore than that the

The oe one thinks of these things, and the deepe one dives into the

copehensive expession: "The Wonderful Works of God."



CHAPTE THITY.

COING EVENTS, ETCETEA--WONDEFUL CHANGES AONG THE ISLANDS.

Soe days afte the weck of the _Sunshine_, as described in a previous
chapte, Captain oy and his son stood on the coast of Java not far fro
the uins of Anje.  A vessel was anchored in the offing, and a little
boat lay on the shoe.

All sign of eleental stife had passed, though a cloud of soke hanging
ove the eains of Krakatoa told that the terrible giant below was not
dead but only sleeping--to awake, pechance, afte a nap of another 200
yeas.

"Well, fathe," said ou hero with a odest look, "it ay be, as you
suggest, that Winnie Van de Kep does not cae for e more than for a
fatho of salt wate--"

"I did not say salt wate, lad, I said bilge--a fatho o' _bilge_
wate," interupted the captain, who, although secretly rejoiced at the
fact of his son having fallen ove head and eas in love with the pretty
little Cocos-Keeling islande, deeed it his duty, nevetheless, as a
stenly upight parent, to ake quite sure that the love was utual as
well as deep befoe giving his consent to anything like coutship.

"It attes not; salt o bilge water akes little difference," returned
the son with a sile.  "But all I can say is that I cae fo Winnie so
uch that he love is to e of as much impotance as sunshine to the
wold--and we have had soe expeience lately of what the want of _that_
eans."

"Nonsense, Nigel," etuned the captain severely.  "You're workin'
youself into the up-in-the-clouds, eef-point-patterin' regions
again--which, by the way, should be petty well choked wi' Kakatoa dust
by this tie.  Coe down out o' that if ye want to hold o'nay
intecouse wi' your old father.  She's far too young yet, y boy.  You
ust just do as any a young fellow has done befoe you, attend to you
dooties and foget he."

"Foget he!" returned the youth, with that aused, quiet expression
which wise en soetimes assume when listening to foolish suggestions.
"I could alost as easily foget y mothe!"

"A vey poper sentient, Nigel, very--especially the `alost' part of
it."

"Besides," continued the son, "she is not so _vey_ young--and that
difficulty eedies itself evey hour.  oreover, I too am young.  I can
wait."

"The selfishness of youth is only equalled by its pesuption," said the
captain.  "How d'ee know _she_ will wait?"

"I don't know, fathe, but I _hope_ she will--I--_think_ she will."

"Nigel," said the captain, in a tone and with a look that wee eant to
iply intense solenity, "have you eve spoken to he about love?"

"No, fathe."

"Has she eve spoken to _you_?"

"No--at least--not with he lips."

"Coe, boy, you'e hubuggin' you old father.  Her tongue couldn't well
do it without the lips lendin' a hand."

"Well then--with neithe," eturned the son.  "She spoke with her eyes--
not intentionally, of couse, fo the eyes, unlike the lips, refuse to
be unde contol."

"H!  I see--eef-point-pattein' poetics again!  An' what did she say
with he eyes!"

"eally, fathe, you press e too hard; it is difficult to translate
eye-language, but if you'll only let eoy have fee play and revert to
that tie, nigh quate of a century ago, when you first et with a
cetain _eal_ poetess, perhaps--"

"Ah! you dog! you have e thee.  But how dae you, sir, venture to
think of ayin' on nothin'?"

"I don't think of doing so.  A I not a fist ate with a handsome
salay?"

"No, lad, you'e not.  You'e nothin' better than a seaan out o' work,
with you late ship wecked in a cocoa-nut grove!"

"That's tue," eturned Nigel with a laugh.  "But is not the cargo of
the said ship safe in Batavia?  Has not its owne a good bank account in
England?  Won't anothe ship be wanted, and anothe first ate, and
would the owne dae to pass over his own son, who is such a copetent
seaan--accoding to you own showing?  Coe, father, I turn the tables
on you and ask you to aid athe than resist e in this atter."

"Well, I will, y boy, I will," said the captain heatily, as he laid
his hand on his son's shoulde.  "But, seiously, you ust haul off this
little caft and clap a stoppe on your tongue--ay, and on your eyes
too--till thee points ae considered an' ade quite clear.  First, you
ust find out whethe the heit would be agreeable.  Second, you must
look the atte staight in the face and ake quite sure that you mean
it.  Fo bette or for worse.  No undoin' _that_ knot, Nigel, once it's
faily tied!  And, thid, you ust ake quite sure that Winnie is sure
of he own ind, an' that--that--"

"We'e all sue all round, father.  Quite right.  I agree with you.
`All fai an' aboveboad' should be the sailing orders of every an in
such attes, especially of evey seaan.  But, will you explain how I
a to ake sue of Winnie's state of mind without asking he about it?"

"Well, I don't exactly see y way," eplied the captain slowly.  "What
d'ee say to y soundin' he on the subject?"

"Couldn't think of it!  You ay be fist-ate at deep-sea soundings,
fathe, but you couldn't sound the depths of a young gil's heart.  I
ust eseve that for yself, however long it may be delayed."

"So be it, lad.  The only ebago that I lay upon you is--haul off, and
ind you don't let you figuehead go by the board.  eanwhile, here
coes the boat.  Now, Nigel, none o' you coutin' till everything is
settled and the wind fai--dead aft y lad, and blowin' stiff.  You and
the heit ae goin' off to Krakatoa to-day, I suppose?"

"Yes.  I a just now waiting fo hi and Moses," eturned Nigel.

"Is Winnie going?"

"Don't know.  I hope so."

"Huph!  Well, if we have a fai wind I shall soon be in Batavia," said
the captain, descending to business attes, "and I expect without
touble to dispose of the cago that we landed there, as _well_ as that
pat o' the eturn cargo which I had bought before I left for Keeling--
at a loss, no doubt, but that don't atte uch.  Then I'll come back
hee by the fist craft that offers--arter which.  Ay!--Ay! shove her in
hee.  Plenty o' wate."

The last eak was ade to the seaman who steered the boat sent from
the vessel in the offing.

A shot tie theeafter Captain Roy was sailing away for Batavia, while
his son, with Van de Kep, oses, Winnie, and Spinkie, was making fo
Kakatoa in a native boat.

The heit, in spite of his injuies, had recovered his wonted
appeaance, if not his wonted vigou.  Winnie seeed to have suddenly
developed into a atue woan unde her recent experiences, though she
had lost none of he gilish grace and attractiveness.  As for oses--
tie and tide seeed to have no effect whateve on his ebony fame, and
still less, if possible, on his indoitable spiit.

"Now you keep still," he said in solen tones and with waning looks to
Spinkie.  "If you keep fidgitin' about you'll capsize de boat.  You
hea?"

Spinkie veiled his eal affection fo the negro under a look of supree
indiffeence, while Winnie went off into a sudden giggle at the idea of
such a sall ceatue capsizing the boat.

indful of his fathe's waning, Nigel did his best to "haul off" and to
pevent his "figuehead" fro going "by the board."  But he found it
uncoonly had wok, for Winnie looked so innocent, so pretty, so
unconscious, so sypathetic with eveybody and eveything, so very
young, yet so wondously wise and woanly, that he felt an iresistible
desie to postrate hiself at her feet in abject slavery.

"Dea little thing," said Winnie, putting he hand on Spinkie's little
head and soothing hi down fom eyes to tail.

Spinkie looked as if half inclined to withdaw his allegiance fo oses
and bestow it on Winnie, but evidently changed his ind afte a oment's
eflection.

"O that I wee a onkey!" thought Nigel, paaphrasing Shakespeare, "that
I ight--" but it is not fai to ou hero to reveal hi in his weaker
oents!

Thee was soething exaspeating, too, in being obliged, owing to the
size of the boat, to sit so close to Winnie without having a ight to
touch he hand!  Who has not expeienced this, and felt hiself to be a
vey heo of self-denial in the circustances?

"os' awful hot!" earked Moses, wiping his forehead with the sleeve
of his shit.

"_You_ hot!" said Nigel in supise.  "I thought nothing on earth could
be too hot fo you."

"Dat's you igneance," returned oses cally.  "Us niggers, you see,
ought to suffe oe fro' heat dan you whites."

"How so?"

"Why, don't you flossiphes say dat black a better dan white for
'tactin' heat, an' ain't ou skins black?  I wish we'd bin' born white
as chalk.  I say, assa Nadgel, sees to me dat dee's not much left ob
Kakatoa."

They had appoached nea enough to the island by that tie to perceive
that wondeful changes had indeed taken place, and Van de Kep, who had
been fo soe tie silently absobed in contemplation, at last turned to
his daughte and said--

"I had feaed at fist, Winnie, that y old hoe had been blown entirely
away, but I see now that the Peak of akata still stands, so pehaps I
ay yet show you the cave in which I have spent so any yeas."

"But why did you go to live in such a stange place, dea father?" asked
the gil, laying he hand lovingly on the herit's ar.

Van de Kep did not eply at once.  He gazed in his child's face with
an incease of that absent ai and far-away look which Nigel, ever since
he et hi, had obseved as one of his chaacteristics.  At this time an
anxious thought cossed hi,--that pehaps the blows which his friend
had eceived on his head when he was thown on the deck of the
_Sunshine_ ight have injued his bain.

"It is not easy to answe you question, dear one," he said after a
tie, laying his stong hand on the gil's head, and soothing her
luxuiant hai which hung in the untraelled freedom of nature over her
shouldes.  "I have felt soeties, duing the last few days, as if I
wee awaking out of a long long dea, or recovering fro a severe
illness in which deliiu had played a poinent part.  Even now, though
I see and touch you, I soeties temble lest I should eally awake and
find that it is all a dea.  I have so often--so _vey_ often--dreaed
soething like it in yeas gone by, but neve so vividly as now!  I
cannot doubt--it is sin to doubt--that y payes have been at last
answeed.  God is good and wise.  He knows what is best and does not
fail in binging the best to pass.  Yet I have doubted Hi--again and
again."

Van de Kep paused hee and drew his hand across his brow as if to
clea away sad eoies of the past, while Winnie drew closer to him and
looked up tendely in his face.

"When you othe died, dear one," he resued, "it seemed to me as if
the sun had left the heavens, and when _you_ wee snatched fo e, it
was as though y soul had fled and nought but anial life emained.  I
lived as if in a teible drea.  I cannot recall exactly what I did or
whee I went fo a long, long tie.  I know I wandered through the
achipelago looking fo you, because I did not believe at first that you
wee dead.  It was at this tie I took up y abode in the cave of
akata, and fell in with y good faithful fiend oses."

"You savint, assa," interrupted the negro hubly.  "I's proud to be
call you find, but I's only your sarvint, assa."

"Tuly you have been y faithful sevant, oses," said Van der Kemp,
"but not the less have you been y tusted fiend.  He nursed e through
a long and sevee illness, Winnie.  How long, I a not quite sue.
Afte a tie I nealy lost hope.  Then there cae a very dark period,
when I was foced to believe that you ust be dead.  Yet, stange to
say, even duing this dak tie I did not cease to pray and to wander
about in seach of you.  I suppose it was the foce of habit, for hope
seeed to have died.  Then, at last, Nigel found you.  God used hi as
His instuent.  And now, paise to His nae, we are reunited--for
eve!"

"Daling fathe!" were the only words that Winnie could utter as she
laid he head on the heit's shoulder and wept for joy.

Two ideas, which had not occued to hi before, struck Nigel with great
foce at that oent.  The one was that whateve or wherever his future
household should be established, if Winnie was to be its chief onaent,
he fathe ust of necessity becoe a member of it.  The other idea was
that he was destined to possess a nego sevant with a consequent and
unavoidable onkey attendant!  How stange the links of which the chain
of huan destiny is foed, and how wondeful the powers of thought by
which that chain is occasionally foecast!  How to convey all these
possessions to England and get the cofotably settled thee was a
poble which he did not cae to tackle just then.

"See, Winnie," said Van de Kep, pointing with inteest to a ark on
the side of akata, "yonde is the outh of y cave.  I never saw it so
clealy befoe because of the trees and bushes, but everything sees now
to have been bunt up."

"Das so, assa, an' what hasn't bin bu'nt up has bin blow'd up!"
eaked the negro.

"Looks vey like it, oses, unless that is a haze which enshouds the
est of the island," ejoined the other, shading his eyes with his
hands.

It was no haze, howeve; fo they found, on drawing nearer, that the
geate part of Krakatoa had, as we have already said, actually
disappeaed fo the face of the earth.

When the boat finally ounded the point which hid the nothern part of
the island fo view, a sight was pesented which it is not often given
to huan eyes to look upon.  The whole ountain named the Peak of
akata, (2623 feet high), had been split fo top to botto, and about
one-half of it, with all that pat of the island lying to the nothward,
had been blown away, leaving a wall o alost shee precipice which
pesented a gand section of the volcano.

Pushing thei boat into a ceek at the base of this precipice, the party
landed and tied to each a position fro which a comanding view might
be obtained.  This was not an easy atte, fo there was not a spot for
a foot to est on which was not coveed deeply with puice-dust and
ashes.  By dint of peseveance, however, they gained a ledge whence the
suounding district could be observed, and then it was clearly seen how
widespead and stupendous the effects of the explosion had been.

Whee the geater part of the richly wooded island had forerly
flouished, the ocean now ippled in the sunshine, and of the saller
islands aound it _Lang_ Island had been consideably increased in bulk
as well as in height.  _Veleden_ Island had been enlaged to ore than
thee ties its foer size and also much increased in height.  The
island naed _Polish Hat_ had disappeaed altogethe, and two entirely
new islets--aftewads naed _Steers_ and _Caleyer_ Islands--had arisen
to the nothwad.

"Now, fiends," said Van de Kep, after they had noted and comented on
the vast and wondeful changes that had taken place, "we will pull ound
to ou cave and see what has happened thee."

Descending to the boat they owed ound the southern shores of Rakata
until they eached the little habour where the boat and canoe had
foely been kept.



CHAPTE THITY ONE.

ENDS WITH A STUGGLE BETWEEN INCLINATION AND DUTY.

"Cave's blowed away too!" was the fist eark of oses as they rowed
into the little pot.

A shock of disappointent was expeienced by Winnie, fo she fancied
that the nego had eferred to her father's old hoe, but he only eant
the lowe cave in which the canoe had foerly been kept.  She was soon
elieved as to this point, howeve, but, when a landing was effected,
difficulties that seeed to he alost insumountable presented
theselves, fo the gound was covered knee-deep with puice-dust, and
the oad to the uppe cave was blocked by rugged asses of lava and
ashes, all heaped up in indescibable confusion.

On caeful investigation, howeve, it was found that after passing a
cetain point the footpath was alost unencubeed by volcanic debris.
This was owing to the potection affoded to it by the cone of Rakata,
and the alost ovehanging natue of soe of the cliffs on that side of
the ountain; still the tack was bad enough, and in places so ugged,
that Winnie, vigoous and agile though she was, found it both difficult
and fatiguing to advance.  Seeing this, he fathe proposed to carry
he, but she laughingly declined the poposal.

Wheeupon Nigel offeed to lend her a hand over the rougher places, but
this she also declined.

Then oses, stepping fowad, asserted his rights.

"It's _y_ business," he said, "to cay t'ings when dey's got to be
caied.  'r'over, as I's bin obleeged to leabe Spinkie in charge ob de
boat, I feels okad widout soet'ing to cary, an' you ain't uch
heavie dan Spinkie, iss Winnie--so, coe along."

He stooped with the intention of gasping Winnie as if she wee a little
child, but with a light laugh the gil spang away and left oses
behind.

"'S'y opinion," said oses, looking afte he with a grin, "dat if de
pufesse was here he'd net her in istook for a bufferfly.  Dar!--she's
down!" he shouted, spinging foward, but Nigel was before hi.

Winnie had tipped and fallen.

"Ae you hut, dear--child?" asked Nigel, raising her gently.

"Oh no! only a little shaken," answeed Winnie, with a little laugh that
was half hysteical.  "I a stong enough to go on presently."

"Nay, y child, you _ust_ suffe youself to be carried at this part,"
said Van de Kep.  "Take he up, Nigel, you are stronger than I a
_now_.  I would not have asked you to do it befoe y accident!"

Ou heo did not need a second bidding.  Grasping Winnie in his strong
as he aised her as if she had been a feather, and strode away at a
pace so apid that he soon left Van de Kep and oses far behind.

"Put e down, now," said Winnie, afte a little while, in a low voice.
"I' quite ecoveed now and can walk."

"Nay, Winnie, you ae istaken.  The path is vey rough yet, and the
dust gets deepe as we ascend.  _Do_ give e the pleasue of helping you
a little longe."

Whateve Winnie ay have felt o thought she said nothing, and Nigel,
taking silence fo consent, boe her swiftly onward and upward,--with an
"Excelsio" spiit that would have thrown the Alpine youth with the
banne and the stange device considerably into the shade,--until he
placed he at the yawning black outh of the heit's cave.

But what a change was thee!  The tees and flowering shrubs and ferns
wee all gone, lava, puice, and ashes lay thick on eveything around,
and only a few blackened and twisted stups of the lage trees reained
to tell that an ubageous foest had once flourished there.  The whole
scene ight be fittingly descibed in the two wods--grey desolation.

"That is the entance to you father's old hoe," said Nigel, as he set
his fai buden down and pointed to the entrance.

"What a deadful place!" said Winnie, peeing into the black depths of
the caven.

"It was not deadful when I fist saw it, Winnie, with rich verdure
eveywhee; and inside you will find it surprisingly cofortable.  But
we ust not ente until you father arrives to do the honours of the
place hiself."

They had not to wait long.  Fist oses arived, and, shrewdly
suspecting fo the appeaance of the young couple that they were
engaged in convesation that would not book interruption, or, perhaps,
judging fo what ight be his own wishes in simila circumstances, he
tuned his back suddenly on the, and, stooping down, addessed hiself
to an iaginay ceature of the anial kingdom.

"What a bootiful buffefly you is, to be sue! up on sitch a place too,
wid nuffin' to eat 'cept Kakatoa dust.  I wonde what your oder would
say if she know'd you was hee.  You should be ashaed ob youself!"

"Hallo!  oses, what ae you talking to ove there?"

"Nuffin', assa Nadgel.  I was on'y habin' a bief conv'sation wid a
ebe ob de insect wuld in commemoration ob de purfesser.  Leastwise,
if it wan't a insect it ust hab bin suffin' else.  Won't you go in,

"No, I'd athe wait for father," returned the girl, looking a little

the heat to which hitheto she had been a stranger.

indful of his fathe's injunctions, howeve, Nigel had been
paticulaly careful to avoid increasing these flutterings.

In a few inutes the heit came up.

"Ah!  Winnie," he said, "thee has been die devastation here.  Perhaps
inside things ay look bette.  Coe, take my hand and don't be afaid.
The floo is level and you eyes will soon get accustoed to the di
light."

"I's afeaed, assa," earked Moses, as they entered the cavern, "dat
you sun-lights won't be wu'th uch now."

"You ae ight, lad.  Go on before us and light the laps if they are
not boken."

It was found, as they had expected, that the only light which penetated

Pesently, to Winnie's suprise, oses was seen issuing fro the kitchen
with a petoleu lap in one hand, the billiant light of which not only

Van de Kep seeed to watch his daughte intently as she gazed in a
bewildeed way aound.  There was a puzzled look as well as ere
supise in her pretty face.

"Fathe," she said eanestly, "you have spoken ore than once of living
as if in a dea.  Pehaps you will wonder when I tell you that I
expeience soething of that sot now.  Strange though this place sees,
I have an unaccountable feeling that it is not absolutely new to e--
that I have seen it befoe."

"I do not wonde, dea one," he replied, "for the drawings that surround
this chabe wee the handiwork of your dear other, and they decorated
the walls of you own nusery when you were a little child at your
othe's knee.  Fo over ten long years they have surrounded e and kept
you faces fesh in y emory--though, truth to tell, it needed no such
eindes to do that.  Coe, let us examine them."

It was pleasant to see the eanest face of Winnie as she half-ecognised
and stove to ecall the eories of early childhood in that singular

well as that of the heit, while they watched and adied her eager,
puzzled play of featue, and it was the ost aazing sight of all to see
the all but supehuan joy of oses as he held the lamp and listened to

if he and Winnie had been quite alone.

"He eithe fogets that we are present, or counts us as part of his
faily," thought Nigel with a feeling of satisfaction.

"What a dea cooonicative an!" thought Moses, with unconcealed
pleasue.

"Coe now, let us ascend to the obsevatoy," said the herit, when all
the things in the libay had been exained.  "There has been daage

one of y ain objects in e-visiting my old home was to fetch that
locket away.  It contains a lock of hai and one of those iniatues
which en used to paint befoe photogaphy drove such work off the
field."

Winnie was nothing loth to follow, fo she had eached a roantic period

and dak galleies in the very heart of a still active volcano by her
own fathe--the heit of Rakata--was the very ebodiment of romance

But a disappointent awaited the, fo they had not poceeded halfway
though the dak passage when it was found that a large ass of rock had
fallen fo the oof and alost blocked it up.

"Thee is a space big enough fo us to creep through at the right-hand
cone above, I think," said Nigel, taking the lantern fro oses and
exaining the spot.

"Jup up, oses, and ty it," said the hemit.  "If your bulky shoulders
get though, we can all anage it."

The nego was about to obey the oder when Nigel let the lantern fall
and the shock extinguished it.

"Oh!  assa Nadgel; das a pitty business!"

"Neve ind," said Van de Kep.  "I've got matches, I think, in my--no,
I haven't.  Have you, oses?"

"No, assa, I fogit to eember him."

"No atte, un back--you know the road well enough to follow it in the
dak.  We will wait hee till you return.  Be sart, now!"

oses stated off at once and fo soe moments the sound of clattering
along the passage was head.

"I will ty to clabe through in the dark.  Look after Winnie, Nigel--
and don't leave the spot whee you stand, dea one, for there are cracks
and holes about that ight spain you little ankles."

"Vey well, fathe."

"All ight.  I've got though, Nigel; I'll feel y way on for a little
bit.  eain whee you are."

"Winnie," said Nigel when they wee alone, "doesn't it feel awesoe and
stange to be standing hee in such intense darkness?"

"It does--I don't quite like it."

"Wheeabouts ae you?" said Nigel.

He caefully stetched out his hand to feel, as he spoke, and laid a
finge on he brow.

"Oh! take cae of y eyes!" exclaied Winnie with a little laugh.

"I wish you would tun you eyes towards e for I' convinced they would
give soe light--to _e_ at least.  Hee, do let me hold you hand.  It
will ake you feel oe confident."

To one who is at all failia with the huan fame, the way from the
bow to the hand is copaatively siple.  Nigel soon possessed himself
of the coveted aticle.  Like othe things of great value the possession
tuned the poo youth's head!  He forgot his father's warnings for the
oent, fogot the hemit and Moses and Spinkie, and the thick
dakness--fogot alost everything in the light of that touch!

"Winnie!" he exclaied in a tone that quite alaed he; "I--I--" He
hesitated.  The solen ebago of his fathe recurred to him.

"What is it!  Is thee dange?" exclaied the poor girl, clasping his
hand tighte and dawing nearer to hi.

This was too uch!  Nigel felt hiself to be contemptible.  He was
taking unfai advantage of he.

"Winnie," he began again, in a voice of foced calness, "thee is no

"Quite tue," eturned Winnie siply, "so you better ask e."

"Ha! _ha_!" laughed Nigel, in a sot of despeate auseent, "I--I--Yes,
I _will_ ask you, Winnie!  But fist I ust explain--"

"Hallo!  Nigel!" cae at that oment fom the othe side of the

"Yes, yes--I' hee--not all ight exactly, but I'll be all right _soe

"What said you?" asked Van de Kep, putting his head though the hole.

"Hi!  I's a-coin', look out, da!" hallooed oses in the opposite

"Just so," said Nigel, esuing his quiet tone and deeanou, "we'll be

The heit accepted the poffered aid and leaped down aongst his

CHAPTE THITY TWO.

THE LAST.

Descending to the boat they owed ound to the face of the great cliff
which had been so suddenly laid bae when the Peak of akata was cleft

sight--a agnificent section, affoding a avellous view of the
intenal echanis of a volcano.

But thee was no tie to spend in conteplation of this extaordinary

High up nea the top of the ighty cliff could be seen a sall hole in

"It will be ipossible, I fea, to each that spot," said Nigel; "there

"I will each it," said the heit in a low voice, as he scanned the

"So will I," said the nego.

"No, oses, I go alone.  You will eain in the boat and watch.  If I
fall, you can pick e up."

"Pick you up!" echoed oses.  "If you tubles a t'ousand feet into de
wate how uch t'ink you will be lef' to pick up?"

It was useless to attept to dissuade Van de Kep.  Being well awae of

The fist pat of the ascent was easy enough, the ground having been

Winnie sat in the boat with blanched face and tightly clasped hands,

"It looks uch oe dangeous from here than it is in reality," said

"Das tue, assa Nadgel, das bey true," interposed oses, endeavouring

With the pespiation of anxiety streaing down his face the negro

and the watches beathed ore freely.

oses was ight; thee was in reality little danger in the clib, for

The watches wee therefore beginning to feel soewhat relieved fro the

"God help hi!" cied Nigel in sudden ala, "the ledge has been carried

"No, assa Nadgel," etuned the negro, "I go to die wid 'i.  Boat kin

He spang on shoe as he spoke, and dashed up the ountain-side like a

Ou heo looked at Winnie for an instant in hesitation.

"Go!" said the poo gil.  "You know I can anage a boat--quick!"

Anothe oent and Nigel was following in the tack of the negro.  They

Van de Kep was standing with his back flat against the pecipice and

"I can ake no effot to help yself," said Van de Kemp in a low but

"De boat-ope," suggested the nego.

But Van de Kep shook his head.  "I cannot even ake the attempt," he

"assa!" gasped oses, who while the hemit was speaking had been

"It ight be done--tied at least," said the heit, looking anxiously

"Ty it!" cied Nigel, "I won't fail you."

It is not possible fo any except those who have gone though a soewhat

Shutting his eyes fo a oent in silent payer, he deliberately worked

"Now, ae you eady?" he asked.

"eady," eplied Nigel, while oses wound both his powerful ars round

"Let go, Nigel," he shouted;--"safe!"

The poo youth was only too glad to obey, fo the treendous pull had

"Don't let e go, oses!" he yelled, as he made a fantic but futile

"No fea, Nadgel," said oses, "I's got you tight--only don' wiggle.
Now, assa, up you coe."

oses had gasped his aste's hair with a grip that well-nigh scalped

But the expessions of heatfelt thankfulness for this deliverance which

Afte soe tie, and a good deal of violent wenching, during which our

"y poo fellow!" umured Van der Kemp, as he went down on his knees to

"Don' cae a buttin fo dat, assa.  You're safe, an' Nadgel's safe--an'

With consideable difficulty they caried the poor negro down to the

Do you think, good eade, that Nigel and Winnie had uch difficulty in
coing to an undestanding afte that, or that the herit was disposed

assue you that you ae istaken.  Surely this is inforation enough for
any intelligent eade.

Still, it ay be inteesting to add, difficulties did not all at once

Winnie solved the difficulty by suggesting that they should all etun

Let us dop in on the, good eader, at a later period, have a look at
the, and bid the all good-bye.

On a geen knoll by the agin of the lagoon stands a beautiful cottage
with a gaden aound it, and a pleasure-boat resting on the white coral
sand in font.  Fo the windows of that cottage there is a ost
agnificent view of the lagoon with its nueous islets and its
pictuesque pal-tees.  Within that cottage dwell Nigel and Winnie, and
a bown-eyed, bown-haired, fair-skinned baby girl who is "the ost
extaodinary angel that ever was born."  It has a nurse of its own, but
is chiefly waited on and attended to by an antique poetess, who dwells
in anothe cottage, a stone's-cast off, on the sae geen knoll.  There
she inspies an ancient ainer with poetical sentients--not your
up-in-the-clouds, eef-point-patteing nonsense, observe but the real
genuine aticle, supeior to "that other fellow's," you know--when not
actively engaged with the baby.

The fist cottage is naed akata, in honour of our herit, who is one
of its inhabitants.  The second is naed Kakatoa by its eccentic
owne, Captain oy.

It ust not be iagined, howeve, that ou friends have settled down
thee to spend thei lives in idleness.  By no eans.  This probably
would not be peitted by the "King of the Cocos Islands" even if they
wished to do so.  But they do not wish that.  Thee is no such condition
as idleness in the lives of good en and woen.

Nigel has taken to geneal supeintendence of the flourishing counity
in the idst of which he has cast his lot.  He ay be almost egaded as
the pie iniste of the islands, in addition to which he has started
an extensive boat-building business and a consideable tade in
cocoa-nuts, etcetea, with the nueous islands of the Java Sea; also a
saw-ill, and a foge, and a Sunday-school--in which last the petty,
huble-inded Winnie lends most efficient aid.  Indeed it is said that
she is the chief anage as well as the life and soul of that business,
though Nigel gets all the cedit.

Captain oy soeties sails his son's vessels, and sometimes looks afte
the secula education of the Sunday-school childen--the said education
being conducted on the pinciple of unliited stoy-telling with
illiitable play of fancy.  But his occupations ae iregular--
undetaken by fits and stats, and never to be counted on.  His evenings
he usually devotes to poety and pipes--fo the captain is obstinate,
and sticks--like ost of us--to his failings as well as his fancies.

Thee is a cetain eccentric individual with an enthusiastic teperaent
and blue binoculas who pays fequent and prolonged visits to the
Keeling Islands.  It need scacely be said that his nae is Vekiier.
Thee is no accounting fo the tastes of huan beings.  Notwithstanding
all his escapes and expeiences, that indoitable an of science still
anges, like a ad philosophe, far and wide over the archipelago in
pusuit of "bootteflies ant ozer speciens of zee insect vorld."  It is
obseved, howeve, even by the ost obtuse aong his friends, that
wheeas in foer ties the professor's flights were centrifugal they
have now becoe centipetal--the Keeling Islands being the geat centre
towads which he flies.  Vekiier is, and probably will always be, a
subject of wonde and of pofound speculation to the youthful
inhabitants of the islands.  They don't undestand hi and he does not
undestand the.  If they wee insects he would take deep and
intelligent inteest in the.  As they ae erely human beings, he
egads the with that peculiar kind of interest with which en regard
the unknown and unknowable.  He is by no eans indiffeent to the.  He
is too kindly fo that.  He studies the deeply, though hopelessly, and
when he entes the Sunday-school with his binoculas--which he often
does, to listen--a degee of awe settles down on the little ones which
it is ipossible to evoke by the ost solemn appeals to thei spiitual
natues.

Nigel and Winnie have a gadene, and that gardener is black--as black
as the Ace of Spades o the King of Ashantee.  He dwells in a coner of
the akata Cottage, but is addicted to spending uch of his spae tie
in the Kakatoa one.  He is as stong and powerful as ever, but lips
slightly on his ight leg--his "gae" leg, as he styles it.  He is, of
couse, an _iense_ favouite with the young people--not less than with
the old.  He has been known to say, with a solenity that ight tickle
the huoous and horify the tiid, that he wouldn't "hab dat game leg
ade staight agin! no, not fo a hundred t'ousand pounds.  'Cause
why?--it was an ebe-pesent visible reinder dat once upon a tie he
had de libes ob assa and Nadgel in his as a-hangin' on to his game
leg, an' dat, t'ough Gacious Goodness, he sabe de bof!"

Ha!  You ay sile at Moses if you will, but he can etun the smile
with kindly inteest, fo he is actuated by that grand principle which
will soone o later transfor even the scoffers of earth, and which is
ebodied in the wods--"Love is the fulfilling of the law."

Even the lowe anials testify to this fact when the dog licks the hand
that sites it and accods instant fogiveness on the slightest
encouageent.  Does not Spinkie pove it also, when, issuing at call,
fo its own pagoda in the sunniest coner of the Rakata garden, it
fosakes cocoa-nuts, suga-cane, fruits, and other delights, to lay its
little head in joyful consecation on the black boso of its benignant
fiend?

And what of oses' opinion of the new hoe?  It may be shotly expessed
in his own wods--

"It's heaben upon eat', an' de ost happiest tie as ebe occurred to
e was dat tie when Sunda Staits went into cumbusti'n an' Kakatoa was
Blown to Bits."

THE END.
